Spotts Unemployment Comp. Case


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


176 Pa. Super. 484; 109 A.2d 212; 1954 Pa. Super. LEXIS 483


September 30, 1954, Argued  
November 9, 1954 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal, No. 218, Oct. T., 1954, by claimant, from decision of Unemployment Compensation Board of Review, dated April 8, 1954, in re claim of Florence Z. Spotts.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employee sought review of a decision from the Pennsylvania Unemployment Compensation Board of Review, which sustained the findings of the bureau and referee and denied unemployment benefits to the employee on the ground that she voluntarily terminated her employment without good cause under the provisions of § 402(b) of the Unemployment Compensation Law.


OVERVIEW: The employee filed an application for unemployment compensation. The record showed that the employee worked the afternoon shift and she received notice of a lay-off due to lack of work on that shift. The employee was notified that work would be available for her on the morning shift. The employee accepted a voluntary lay-off because she had no one to stay with her three small children during the morning shift hours. The employee claimed that she was compelled to cease work, not because of her changed circumstances, but because of the inability of the employer to provide continued employment on the same terms as before. The court affirmed the order of the Unemployment Compensation Board of Review denying the employee's unemployment claim and concluded that the employment relationship was severed by the employee and not by her employer, and her reason for the severance could not be considered a good cause under the express language of the Unemployment Compensation Law. The court ruled that the Law did not violate public policy and held that it was not the legislature's intention to permit an employee to insist upon working a particular shift because of domestic circumstances.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the Pennsylvania Unemployment Compensation Board of Review.

 

677 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Berner v. Pennsylvania Public Utility Com.


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


177 Pa. Super. 19; 107 A.2d 882; 1954 Pa. Super. LEXIS 254


April 20, 1954, Argued  
September 27, 1954 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal, No. 87, Oct. T., 1954, from order of Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission, Application Docket No. 15845, in case of Clyde E. Berner et al., trading as Valley Planing Mill v. Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission and Commonwealth Telephone Company.

Proceeding before the Public Utility Commission upon complaints against proposed increased rates of telephone company.

Order entered dismissing complaints and approving proposed rates. Complainants appealed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant consumer sought review of the order of appellee Public Utility Commission (Pennsylvania), in a complaint lodged by the consumer against a rate increase for local exchange service by the telephone company.


OVERVIEW: The telephone company filed proposed new tariffs providing for rate increases for local exchange service. The rates were suspended by the commission, pending investigation. The consumer filed a complaint against the rate increase. Following hearings, the commission entered an order approving the proposed rates. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that the commission used methods reasonably calculated to produce accurate findings. The commission did not err in refusing to take into account future predictions of increased numbers of telephone subscribers. If future predictions of increased subscribers were considered, then the commission would have had to make corresponding adjustments for estimated future expenses involved in handling the increased subscribers. The commission's methods, based on actual experience and geared to the known costs and income, were not in error. The court also held that the commission did not err in its allowance for materials and supplies. There was no error of law and the findings were supported by competent evidence. Finally, the court found no error in the commission's finding of a fair return.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the commission's order approving new tariffs providing for rate increases for local exchange service for a telephone company, objected to by the consumer.

 

678 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Gibson v. Hallacher


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


176 Pa. Super. 539; 107 A.2d 449; 1954 Pa. Super. LEXIS 443


March 9, 1954, Argued  
August 26, 1954 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal, No. 18, March T., 1954, from order of Court of Common Pleas of York County, April T., 1950, No. 214, in case of Charles T. Gibson, Admr., Estate of Wayne C. Gibson, deceased v. Victor E. Hallacher, Jr.

Trespass for wrongful death. Before Sherwood, P.J.

Verdict for plaintiff for "funeral expenses plus $ 1000", a total of $ 1,387; plaintiff's motion for new trial granted. Defendant appealed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged an order of the Court of Common Pleas of York County (Pennsylvania), which granted plaintiff a new trial in his action in trespass for wrongful death on the ground of the inadequacy of the verdict.


OVERVIEW: The decedent was killed while he was a passenger in an automobile driven by defendant. Plaintiff brought an action of trespass and the jury returned a verdict for plaintiff for the amount of the funeral expenses incurred plus $ 1,000. The trial court granted plaintiff a new trial on the ground of inadequacy of the verdict and the court affirmed. It found that the granting of a new trial for inadequacy of the verdict was a matter for the sound discretion of the trial court and, in the absence of a gross abuse of such discretion, the court would not reverse. Consequently, the question before the court was not whether in its opinion the verdict of the jury was inadequate, but solely whether the trial court committed a gross abuse of discretion in granting a new trial because in its opinion it was inadequate. In this case, considering the decedent's hourly wage and his life expectancy, the court could not find that there was a gross abuse of discretion by the trial court in granting a new trial on the ground of inadequacy of the verdict.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order granting plaintiff a new trial.

 

679 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Wynn


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


175 Pa. Super. 546; 106 A.2d 647; 1954 Pa. Super. LEXIS 357


March 22, 1954, Argued  
July 13, 1954 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal, No. 94, Oct. T., 1954, from judgment of Court of Quarter Sessions of Philadelphia County, August Sessions, 1953, No. 1084, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Charles Wynn and Thomas McDavid.

Indictments charging defendants with burglary, larceny and receiving stolen goods. Before Alessandroni, J., without a jury.

Verdicts of not guilty as to charges of burglary and larceny and guilty as to charge of receiving stolen goods, and judgment of sentence entered thereon. Defendant appealed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Court of Quarter Sessions of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania) that convicted him of receiving stolen goods. The Commonwealth of Pennsylvania filed a motion to quash the appeal as untimely.


OVERVIEW: Defendant took an appeal three months after sentencing, which the Commonwealth sought to quash as untimely because Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 12, § 1136 provided that appeals from a court of quarter sessions had to be taken within 45 days from sentencing. Defendant alleged that the Act of May 11, 1927, P. L. 972 (Pennsylvania), which reduced the time for appeals from the courts of quarter sessions from three months to 45 days, was unconstitutional because the subject of the amending bill was not clearly expressed in its title, as required by Pa. Const. art. III, § 3. The title of the 1927 act stated that it regulated the practice, bail, costs, and fees on appeals to the supreme court and superior court, and fixed the time within which appeals might be allowed and heard. In quashing the appeal, the court held that anyone seeking to determine the time within which an appeal could be taken to the superior court would be expected to examine the body of an act where the title stated that it fixed the time within which appeals might be allowed. The title's reference to "fixing," rather than reducing, the time for appeals was not misleading as fixing could mean reducing, changing, or extending.


OUTCOME: The court quashed the appeal.

 

680 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Coldsmith


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


176 Pa. Super. 283; 106 A.2d 649; 1954 Pa. Super. LEXIS 358


March 8, 1954, Argued  
July 13, 1954 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals, Nos. 13 and 14, March T., 1954, from judgment of Court of Quarter Sessions of Dauphin County, Sept. Sessions, 1952, Nos. 382 and 383, in cases of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Howard Coldsmith; and Same v. Harry Pheil, Jr.

Appeal by defendants from summary convictions by a justice of the peace on charges of speeding in violation of the Pennsylvania Motor Vehicle Code.

Defendants adjudged guilty and judgment of sentence entered, opinion by Kreider, J. Defendants appealed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appeal their summary convictions from the Court of Quarter Sessions of Dauphin County (Pennsylvania) on charges of violation of the Pennsylvania Motor Vehicle Code.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were convicted on charges of speeding under Article X, § 1002(b)(6) of the Pennsylvania Motor Vehicle Code. Defendants moved to quash the informations claiming that they did not set forth that the justice of the peace was the nearest available magistrate or that his office was in the township where the violations occurred. The court affirmed defendants' convictions and concluded that defendants failed in their burden of proving that the justice of the peace where defendants were first taken was in the wrong township or that the justice was the nearest available magistrate. The court held that defendants had no cause to complain regarding the informations since they offered no proof of defectiveness of notice. The court ruled that the Motor Vehicle Code was not unconstitutional and defendants were not entitled to cross-examine the mechanic in charge of adjusting the police officer's vehicles' speedometers for accuracy. The court held that the testimony of a police officer that he timed defendants' cars traveling in the same lane of traffic and the gap between their automobiles remained constant for a sustained period of time was sufficient to sustain the convictions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgments of conviction against defendants.

 

681 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Shaffer


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


175 Pa. Super. 100; 103 A.2d 430; 1954 Pa. Super. LEXIS 298; 42 A.L.R.2d 761


October 9, 1953, Argued  
March 16, 1954 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal, No. 287, Oct. T., 1953, from order of Court of Quarter Sessions of Lehigh County, April Sessions, 1953, No. 47-A in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Morris B. Shaffer.

Proceedings after transmittal of support proceeding in Florida court to court of quarter sessions.

Order entered dismissing support petition and discharging defendant, opinion by Henninger, P.J. Commonwealth appealed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant wife sought review of the order of the Court of Quarter Sessions of Lehigh County (Pennsylvania), which dismissed her support petition and dismissed respondent husband for lack of jurisdiction.


OVERVIEW: The wife filed a petition for support in a Florida court, which transferred the case to the lower court in Pennsylvania. The husband filed a motion to dismiss for lack of jurisdiction because (1) reciprocal operation of the Pennsylvania law, Penn. Stat. Ann. tit. 62, 2043.1 et seq., could not be invoked because it is not substantially similar to the law of Florida, Fla. Stat. ch. 27996 (1953); (2) there was no authority in either the Florida or the Pennsylvania law for the arrest of respondent on a bench warrant; (3) the Pennsylvania Law was unconstitutional in that it contained no provision for confrontation of accusing witnesses including his wife. The lower court granted the motion. On appeal, the court reversed and held that (1) the lower court had jurisdiction; and (2) the Pennsylvania and Florida statutes were sufficiently similar to allow the reciprocal operation of the spousal support laws. The methods of bringing the complaint before the responding state by the initiating state were substantially parallel, and both acts provided for reciprocal relief.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's order with a procedendo.

 

682 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Magee Carpet Co. v. Pennsylvania Public Utility Com.


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


174 Pa. Super. 438; 102 A.2d 229; 1954 Pa. Super. LEXIS 280


November 17, 1953, Argued  
January 19, 1954 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals, No. 27, March T., 1953, No. 98, Oct. T., 1953 and No. 2, March T., 1954, from order of Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission, August 12, 1952, in case of Magee Carpet Company et al. v. Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission.

Proceedings before Public Utility Commission upon complaints against tariff supplements of power company.

Order entered finding that fuel adjustment clause was unreasonable and directing filing of tariff supplement containing modification; order entered extending time for company to file a tariff containing a clause as directed; order entered denying petitions for rehearing and amending prior order to include finding that refunds were due to certain consumers. Complainants, and power company, respectively, appealed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Complainants, consumers of electric power, sought relief from an electric power utility's tariff supplements. The consumers and the utility appealed from an order of the Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission, which found that the utility's fuel adjustment clause was unreasonable, which directed filing of tariff supplement containing a modification, which found that refunds were due to certain consumers, and which denied a motion for rehearing.


OVERVIEW: The utility filed tariff supplements providing for application of the fuel adjustment clause. After complaints were filed, the Commission ordered refunds to certain consumers because the clause was being applied without regard to the source of the energy supplied to consumers and because it did not adequately provide for improving efficiency. In affirming the order, the court reasoned that the Commission had the power to order refunds and that it properly designated the refund period pursuant to § 313(a) of the Public Utility Law of 1937, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 66, § 1153. The purpose of the clause was to stabilize, not augment, the allowable return of the utility from its basic rates. The clause permitted the utility to recover more than the bare cost of fuel and was applied to consumers of energy that was not produced using fuel. The refund order was proper and lawful. Another order of the Commission regarding payment by industrial consumers was made in a separate and distinct proceeding and did not concern refunds ordered over a prior period in a prior proceeding. Refunds were not mandatory but discretionary with the Commission.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the Commission's order.

 

683 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth ex rel. Clawges v. Claudy


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


173 Pa. Super. 410; 98 A.2d 225; 1953 Pa. Super. LEXIS 445


April 13, 1953, Argued  
July 14, 1953 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal, No. 9, April T., 1953, from judgment of Court of Common Pleas of Butler County, Sept. T., 1951, Misc. Docket No. 6, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania ex rel. George Clawges v. Dr. John W. Claudy, Warden, Western State Penitentiary.

Habeas corpus proceeding.

Order entered dismissing petition, opinion by Purvis, P.J. Relator appealed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Relator inmate challenged the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Butler County (Pennsylvania) that dismissed his petition for a writ of habeas corpus.


OVERVIEW: The inmate pleaded guilty to burglary when he was 19 years old and received a general sentence to an industrial school under the provisions of Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 61, § 485 (1951). The inmate was released on parole one year later and recommitted as a technical violator within two years. A year later, he was re-paroled, and was almost immediately recommitted. The inmate was soon transferred from the industrial school to the penitentiary. He was paroled a year and a half later, and recommitted within three years. Six months later, the inmate filed a petition for a writ of habeas corpus. The trial court dismissed the petition for lack of jurisdiction. Affirming without agreeing with the trial court's reasoning, the court found that the inmate was lawfully confined and that a hearing was unnecessary because only questions of law were presented. Section 485 did not violate due process or equal protection because the statute did not extend the inmate's sentence beyond the maximum allowed for burglary and did not exclude him from the benefit of parole. Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 61, § 331.31 (1943), which allowed parole, applied to the inmate because an industrial school was a penal institution.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of the inmate's petition.

 

684 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Anderson v. Sunray Electric, Inc.


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


173 Pa. Super. 566; 98 A.2d 374; 1953 Pa. Super. LEXIS 460


April 16, 1953, Argued  
July 14, 1953 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal, No. 42, April T., 1953, from judgment of Court of Common Pleas of Warren County, Nov. T., 1952, Nos. 2 and 3, in case of Katherine F. Anderson v. Sunray Electric, Inc., State Workmen's Insurance Fund and Commonwealth of Pennsylvania.

Appeal by Commonwealth and State Insurance Fund from award by Workmen's Compensation Board.

Order entered dismissing appeals, opinion by Wade, P.J. Employer, Commonwealth, and State Insurance Fund appealed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, an employer, its insurer, and the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, challenged a judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Warren County (Pennsylvania), which affirmed a Pennsylvania Workmen's Compensation Board's decision that appellee claimant was totally disabled as a result of beryllium poisoning and was entitled to compensation under the Pennsylvania Occupational Disease Act of June 21, 1939.


OVERVIEW: In considering the matter, the court noted that neither the occupational disease legislation of July 2, 1937, P. L. 2714 (Pennsylvania), nor the Pennsylvania Occupational Disease Act of June 21, 1939, P. L. 566, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 77, § 1201 et seq., nor the 1945 amendments to the Occupational Disease Act, included beryllium poisoning as a compensable occupational disease. The court determined that beryllium poisoning was first introduced into legislation in 1949; the claimant, however, worked for the employer from 1946 to 1947. Accordingly, because that disease was not covered or recognized by law as a compensable occupational disease while the claimant worked for the employer, the court ruled that she was not entitled to compensation. The court reasoned that when an employer and employee accepted the occupational disease legislation, their relation became contractual and the laws became part of the terms of employment. In addition, the court ruled that applying the Act of May 14, 1949, P. L. 1379, § 1, which was effective July 1, 1949, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 77, § 1208, and which introduced the disease, would have impaired the parties' vested contractual rights.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment and entered judgment for appellants.

Werner Appeal


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


173 Pa. Super. 242; 98 A.2d 487; 1953 Pa. Super. LEXIS 504


April 13, 1953, Argued  
July 14, 1953 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal, No. 23, April T., 1953, from order of Court of Common Pleas of Somerset County, 1952, Commonwealth Docket No. 849, in re Annual Report of Auditors of Somerset County for 1951.

Appeal by taxpayers from report of county auditors. Before Lansberry, P.J.

Order entered quashing appeal. Taxpayer appealed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayers challenged an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Somerset County (Pennsylvania) quashing their appeal from a report of county auditors for a certain year. The petition for appeal set forth that it was taken under the Pennsylvania Act of May 2, 1929, P. L. 1278, art. XII, § 1035, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 16, § 1035.


OVERVIEW: The auditors filed a motion to quash and dismiss the appeal because, inter alia, no recognizance with two sufficient sureties, as required by the Act of 1929, had been filed. The court below granted the motion to quash the appeal, as it appeared from the record and was admitted by the taxpayers that there had not been a compliance with the Act of 1929. On appeal, the court held that the proceedings in the court below were fatally defective in that the taxpayers in their appeal from the auditors' report failed to file a recognizance in the manner and form prescribed by the Act of 1929. The court noted that the taxpayers asserted that the county commissioners would be allowed to retain payments which were illegal if the court below was sustained in quashing the appeal from the auditors' report. The court stated, in reply, that payments made out of public funds by public officials to themselves as compensation or expenses, under an authorizing statute which was subsequently declared unconstitutional or inapplicable or void, could, because of public policy, be recovered by the county.


OUTCOME: The order was affirmed.

 

686 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Knox


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


172 Pa. Super. 510; 94 A.2d 128; 1953 Pa. Super. LEXIS 375



January 20, 1953 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction and sentence under an indictment, which charged him with violations of the Magistrates' Court Act of June 15, 1937, P.L.1743, as amended, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 42, § 1101 et seq., malfeasance, misfeasance and nonfeasance, and misconduct in office, from the trial court (Pennsylvania).


OVERVIEW: Defendant violated the provisions of the Act, which required him to have had real estate bail justified under oath, to have secured a controller's certificate, to have inquired into the sufficiency of offered bail, and to have compelled production of the muniments of title before he released persons on bail. The court rejected defendant's argument that the Act was unconstitutional. The Act did not interfere with, restrain, coerce, or diminish the judicial power. It was a valid exercise of police power by the legislature, which was intended to correct evils that existed in the magistrate courts. However, defendant's convictions under the common law counts of malfeasance and misconduct were reversed because public officials could not be convicted of such offenses where a statute provided a specific penalty for such violations. The Act provided that the magistrate was to have been removed from office, and it was not necessary to have resorted to impeachment or other processes set forth in the Pennsylvania Constitution. The trial court's charges were correct, and evidence of good character was properly rejected as it related to violations of the Act because no mens rea was involved.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's convictions for malfeasance, misfeasance and nonfeasance, and misconduct in office, but affirmed the remainder of the convictions, the judgment of the trial court, and defendant's sentence.

 

687 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


School Dist. v. Star Theater, Inc.


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


172 Pa. Super. 291; 94 A.2d 53; 1953 Pa. Super. LEXIS 342



January 20, 1953 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant amusement vendors challenged judgments from the lower court (Pennsylvania), which sustained the validity of appellee school district's ordinance and entered judgments against the amusement vendors for the admission taxes they owed. The amusement vendors contended that the ordinance was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The school district enacted the disputed ordinance under 53 P.S. § 2015.1 et seq. and imposed a 10 percent tax on amusement admissions. The ordinance exempted certain groups and had a severability clause. The amusement vendors operated commercial enterprises that charged admissions and were not part of the exempt group. The lower court concluded that the exemption clause was discriminatory, based on improper classification, and not within the exemption to charities under Pa. Const. art. IX, § 1, but held that, under the severability clause, the act had to be read without the provision, and the nontaxables then became part of the taxable class. The lower court maintained that the exempted organizations would be included if necessary to sustain the ordinance's validity, so it was valid as to the amusement vendors. On review, the court declined to decide the ordinance's constitutionality because the exemption clause had already been assumed invalid. The court concluded that the severability clause, in effect, struck down the exempting provision, made the nontaxables part of the taxable class, and rendered the ordinance valid and enforceable as to the amusement vendors.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgments against the amusement vendors for the admission taxes they owed under the school district ordinance.

 

688 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth ex rel. Williamson v. Burke


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


172 Pa. Super. 39; 92 A.2d 239; 1952 Pa. Super. LEXIS 453



November 12, 1952 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate appealed from an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Lycoming County (Pennsylvania), which dismissed his petition for habeas corpus relief. He had been sentenced to an industrial school after pleading guilty to three indictments charging burglary and larceny. The first time he was paroled he pleaded guilty and served time on a charge of burglary. He was twice more paroled and recommitted as a technical parole violator.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the court held than none of the inmate's contentions had merit. The facts were not in dispute. In large part, his contentions had been decided adversely to him by prior decisions of the court. Others were determined by statute. Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 61, § 331.17 gave exclusive power and jurisdiction on all matters of parole to the Pennsylvania Board of Parole. That power extended to those sentenced to the industrial school. Because the Parole Board's power was exclusive, no act or statement by the authorities of the industrial school or the trial judge could change or terminate the general sentence for burglary that automatically subjected him to confinement at the school for a term of 20 years, the maximum punishment that could be imposed for the crime pursuant to Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 18, § 4901. Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 61, §§ 485 and 495 were not unconstitutional because they did not attempt to delegate judicial power to the Parole Board in contravention of Pa. Const. art. V, § 1. The granting or withholding of credit against a sentence for time spent on parole was discretionary on the part of the Parole Board.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's dismissal of the inmate's petition for habeas corpus.

 

689 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Franklin


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


172 Pa. Super. 152; 92 A.2d 272; 1952 Pa. Super. LEXIS 468



November 12, 1952 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1]  [***1] The following is the opinion of Judge Milner in the court below:

There is before us for consideration a rule granted upon the district attorney to show cause why a recognizance demanded of the petitioner should not be quashed and discharged.

It is stated in the amended petition that Edward J. Franklin, the petitioner, was indicted for assault and battery and for unlawfully resisting arrest, as of December Term, 1950, No. 347, in the Court of Quarter Sessions of the Peace for Philadelphia County. The matter came on for trial before a judge and jury and the petitioner was found not guilty. The trial judge, nevertheless, thereupon ordered that the defendant be 'held in $ 1,000.00 bail to keep the peace for a period of two (2) years. Defendant permitted to sign own bond,' and the order was duly endorsed upon the bill of indictment upon which petitioner had been acquitted. The petitioner that day entered bail so that he would be committed for failure to enter such bond.

The amended petition sets forth, and the district attorney has not controverted, that 'neither the commonwealth nor others had produced evidence upon which there could be found that the defendant [***2] had a previous [***2] criminal record or had been previously indicted for offenses other than those on which he had been that day acquitted, or that the defendant had the reputation of being a dangerous character in any respect, or that petitioner was 'not of good fame'.' We have examined the record of the trial of defendant Franklin and can find no evidence that he had a reputation of being a dangerous character in any respect or that he was not of good fame. The petitioner contends that the trial judge did not have the power or authority to order him held in such bail; that the 'order was without factual or legal justification'; and that the order is in contravention of petitioner's rights under the 13th and 14th amendments to the Constitution of the United States and of Article I, Sections 9, 10 and 13, of the Pennsylvania Constitution, P.S.

Because we are aware that many judges in this state have exercised a similar power to that which we are now called upon to subject to inquiry, and because we regard the matter as involving fundamental civil rights, we shall undertake an extended review of the authority upon which the power to bind after acquittal is alleged to rest. Preliminarily [***3] we must limit [***3] our inquiry by noting that we are not here concerned with the admitted common-law power of a judge to impose as part of a sentence, after conviction, a requirement that a defendant enter security for his good behavior. Nor are we considering the statutory peace bond which may be required of a defendant upon complaint of a third person that such defendant has threatened the person or property of the complainant: see Act of March 31, 1860, P.L. 427, section 6, 19 P.S. § 23, and see also Acts of March 18, 1909, P.L. 42, 19 P.S. §§ 24-26, April 27, 1909, P.S. 260, 19 P.S. §§ 27-28, and see 1 Edw. III, Stat. 2, c. 16 (1327). We are here concerned only with the assertion that a trial judge, following a trial resulting in an acquittal of the accused, may require such defendant to post such security, to assure his good behavior, as the trial judge shall, in his discretion, direct and for such period of time as such judge may in his discretion direct. 1


1.  See the excellent note in 88 U. of Pa.L.Rev. 331 (1940) for a careful review of the practice of requiring bonds in the United States and note the careful distinction maintained throughout between surety to keep the peace and demand of a bond for good behavior; the distinction is also maintained for bonds demanded after conviction and those demanded after acquittal.

 [***4]  [***4] In undertaking this inquiry into the legality of the asserted authority we are aware of the fact that our Supreme Court over a century ago would seem to have already passed upon and confirmed the existence of such power. In such circumstance there would ordinarily remain nothing further for consideration by a lower court but compliance with the law as pronounced by the highest tribunal of this Commonwealth. But, as we shall hereinafter indicate at greater length, none of the judicial authorities relied upon is of even comparatively recent date and there are raised in this proceeding, for the first time 2 constitutional questions of grave import. In the circumstances, we are bound to consider the impact of the adoption of the Fourteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution upon the statutes of this Commonwealth. We have been unable to find any binding authority in this Commonwealth, or, indeed, in any of our sister states, which considered the validity of the ancient English statute of 34 Edw. III, c. 1, in the light of modern constitutional concepts. In Quong Wing v. Kirkendall, 1912, 223 U.S. 59, 64, 32 S.Ct. 192, 193, 56 L.Ed. 350, Mr. Justice [***5] Holmes stated, 'Laws frequently [***5] are enforced which the court recognizes as possibly or probably invalid if attacked by a different interest or in a different way.'


2.  In United States ex rel. Henson v. Mills, E.D.Pa.1937, 21 F.Supp. 616, reported also in 31 Pa.Dist. & Co. 47, 48, a habeas corpus proceeding, Judge Maris reviewed an order, after acquittal, directing entry of bail in the sum of $ 25,000. to keep the peace and be of good behavior for a period of ten years. The question of due process was not thoroughly considered and was not argued. There was dicta (based on an assumption of propriety) which we may ignore. See also the dicta in Commonwealth v. Andrews, 1905, 211 in Commonwealth v. Andrews, 1905, 211 Pa. 110, 112-113, 60 A. 554.

From our survey we have been unable to find any other state in our Union in which it is maintained that a defendant acquitted in a criminal trial can be held under bond to keep the peace. Yet the practice has persisted for many years in Philadelphia County. The Public Defender, in his excellent brief as amicus curiae, states that the Report of the Board of Inspectors of the Philadelphia [***6] County Prison for the years 1939 to 1949 indicates that in that period 478 men,  [***6] after acquittal of criminal charges, were compelled to serve an aggregate of over 600 years in the Philadelphia County Prison in default of bonds aggregating $ 613,200. Hundreds of defendants, acquitted by juries of their peers, have been placed under the restriction of a bond to keep the peace and many who have been unable to furnish bond or have failed to enter their recognizance have languished in jail and some are now in jail as a result of this practice. The authority for this practice is said to lie in an old English Statute adopted six centuries ago. In the first place we believe, from our review of the history of the Statute of 34 Edward III, that neither it nor the cases relating to it, including the Bamber case [Bamber v. Com.] 10 Pa. 339, decided by our Supreme Court over a century ago, justify the practice, and that in any event, since the adoption of the Fourteenth Amendment to the Constitution of the United States in 1868, and the adoption of the present Constitution of Pennsylvania in 1873, the practice is contrary to our fundamental law, is against common sense [***7] and an enlightened sense of justice, and is indefensible.

The Fourteenth Amendment to the Federal Constitution [***7] declares that no state shall deprive any person of life, liberty or property without due process of law. This amendment is a limitation on the powers of the states. 12 Am.Jur., Const.Law, Sect. 567, p. 258. In the chapter on Constitutional Law in American Jurisprudence in Section 573, it is stated:

'The essential elements of due process of law are notice and an opportunity to be heard and to defend in an orderly proceeding adapted to the nature of the case before a tribunal having jurisdiction of the cause. One of the most famous and perhaps the most often quoted definition of due process of law is that of Daniel Webster in his argument in the Dartmouth College Case, [Trustees of Dartmouth College v. Woodward] 4 Wheat 518, 4 L.Ed. 629, in which he declared that by due process of law is meant 'a law which hears before it condemns; which proceeds upon inquiry, and renders judgment only after trial.' Somewhat similar is the statement that it is as old as the law that no one shall be personally bound until he has had his day in court, by which is [***8] meant until he has been duly cited to appear and has been afforded an opportunity to be heard. Judgment without such citation and opportunity [***8] lacks all the attributes of a judicial determination; it is judicial usurpation and oppression and can never be upheld where justice is fairly administered.'

Article I, Sec. 9 of the Constitution of Pennsylvania provides:

'In all criminal prosecutions the accused hath a right to be heard by himself and his counsel, to demand the nature and cause of the accusation against him, to meet the witnesses face to face, to have compulsory process for obtaining witnesses in his favor, and, in prosecution by indictment or information, a speedy public trial by an impartial jury of the vicinage; he cannot be compelled to give evidence against himself, nor can he be deprived of his life, liberty or property, unless by the judgment of his peers or the law of the land.'

In the case before us the petitioner was held under bond to keep the peace without any information or complaint lodged against him charging him with any offense mentioned in the Statute of 34 Edward III, which is set forth later on in this opinion. He was indicted for assault and [***9] battery and found not guilty. He had no notice that he might be accused of being a person 'not of good fame,' or of any other charge which could result [***9] in the restraint of his liberty or freedom. Without a trial by a jury of his peers he found himself adjudged quilty by a judge upon a suspicion of what he might do, and was placed under bond. This restriction was a punishment and deprivation of his rights without due process of law.

The argument has been made here that the practice which we condemn existed at common law in England and in the early days of this Commonwealth. We have said that we do not concede there is good authority for the practice in the early common law, but in any event this argument is answered in American Jurisprudence, Constitutional Law, at Section 580, as follows:

'The fact that one of the tests of due process is the common law as it existed at the time of the establishment of our civil and political institutions does not prevent a change in the law as it then existed. There is no constitutional right to have all general propositions of law once adopted remain unchanged. It is not to be inferred that all modes of procedure which do not have [***10] the sanction of this early law are to be condemned. Nor can it be said that every form of procedure settled in English law at the time of the emigration to America [***10] and brought to this country and practiced by the early colonists is an essential element of due process of law. It has been pertinently remarked that if this were so, the procedure of the first half of the seventeenth century would be fastened upon American jurisprudence like a strait jacket, only to be unloosened by constitutional amendment, and that it would be to deny every quality of the law but its age and to render it incapable of progress or improvement. The guaranty of due process demands only that the law shall not be unreasonable, arbitrary, or capricious, and that the means selected shall have a real and substantial relation to the object sought to be attained.' (The excerpts from American Jurisprudence are fortified by a host of decisions of the Supreme Court of the United States, and the appellate courts of this and other states, referred to in the notes to the above mentioned sections.)

We have concluded that there is no valid authority under the law as it exists today to place the petitioner [***11] under bond. He was acquitted of the charge lodged against him and he is entitled to his complete freedom, unfettered by any bond or restriction.

Because of the importance of [***11] this and the fact that the practice we condemn has apparently been accepted and followed without adequate examination into its legitimacy, we are obliged to re-examine the subject at some length.

The first reported Pennsylvania case which we have found which approved the requirement that defendants post bond after and despite their acquittal, was Respublica v. Donagan, 1799, 2 Yeates 437. In that case Patrick Donagan and Francis Cox, the defendants, were charged as accessories before the fact to murder and were found not guilty. The court ordered each to 'give security to keep the peace, and be of good behavior to all the liege citizens of the United States, and in particular to Peter Shitz, for the term of fourteen years, each in the sum of 10,000 dollars and two good sureties in 10,000 dollars each.'

The report states that 'there was strong reason to believe that the prisoners had been concerned in a most horrid murder, though there was not sufficient evidence to convict them of the [***12] crime. The court directed them to give the above security, under the firm opinion that they were persons of most dangerous character, and not being able to give the security, they had remained in [***12] gaol.'

On a motion for their enlargement it was contended that the orders operated as life sentences; that the constitution forbade requirement of excessive bail, imposition of excessive fines or imposition of cruel punishments. The motion was denied per curiam on the statement that 'The court before whom the trial was had, under their general authority to preserve the peace, had a right to require such bail and for such a length of time, as they judged would best answer the ends of public justice. No doubt can be entertained of it. And it would be highly improper for us to interfere, in a matter wherein they have exercised their legal discretion. Unsafe would the community be, if such characters could prowl at large through the country, without a sufficient tie on them.' By footnote, reference was made, not by the court, but by the editor, to the following English authorities, viz.:

'Surety for the good behavior ordered by the Court after acquittal. Comb. 40, 2 Hawk. 442.  [***13] 3 One may be bound with sureties for his good behaviour even during life. Cr. Car. 332, 378. 2 Stra. 834, 1 Hawk. 106, 129.


3.  This reference to Sergeant Hawkins we shall consider infra. The reference to 'Comb. 40' is to Rex v. Sir John Knight and is also discussed infra.

 [***13] 'Surety of the peace may be demanded for any number of years that the public safety requires. It is discretionary in the court. 1 Term, Rep. 700'.

A note following the opinion states, 'The prisoner afterwards broke gaol and escaped.'

Apparently the next case to arise in this Commonwealth was Respublica v. Cobbet, 1800, 3 Yeates 91, where the defendant caused publication of certain allegedly slanderous words as to the governments and officers of the United States and this Commonwealth. A recognizance in stated amounts was taken by Thomas McKean, the then chief justice 'conditioned that if the above bounden William Cobbett, shall be of good behavior towards the aforesaid Commonwealth, and all the liege people until the next Court * * * then the said recognizance to be void, otherwise to remain in force.' The proceeding was in debt on forfeited recognizance for good [***14] behavior. Although there was reference to the statute of 34 Edw. III, c. 1 and the right to require surety prior to conviction there is nothing to indicate that such binding to good behavior followed an acquittal. It would seem that the action must have originated on complaint of some third person, a procedure not [***14] here challenged.

The next 4 and perhaps most important Pennsylvania case was Bamber v. Commonwealth, 1849, 10 Pa. 339, in which two defendants were indicted for burglaries acquitted in the Court of Quarter Sessions of Philadelphia. The following order was then recorded, 'And now, &c., from the evidence presented to the court in the trial of this cause, the court order that the defendants, &c., give bail in the sum of $ 5,000. to keep the peace and be of good behaviour to all good citizens of this Commonwealth for the term of one year each; and that they stand committed until this order is complied with.' The judgment was affirmed per curiam without opinion and the reported proceeding indicates that both Coulter, J. and Rogers, J. relied on the vague assumption that such power was said to have thitherto been undoubted. Counsel for the appellant [***15] argued as follows, 'The order is, to find surety of the peace and for good behaviour. The former is under the Act of 1700, and can only be on the oath of one that he goeth in bodily fear of another. The latter is founded on 34 Ed. 3, c. 1, and ought to be conditioned for the party's appearance to answer, otherwise what becomes of the [***15] provision in Mag. Ch. Nullus liber homo capiatur vel imprisonetur * * * nisi per legale judicium paricum suorum vel per legem terrae. 5 2 Hale, 136; 2 B. & Ald. 288 [Willes v. Bridger, 106 Eng.Rep. 368 (K.B.1819)]. Though it is true the justices have discretionary power to require surety, their discretion is to be exercised according to the law of the land, and not by their private notions: 5 Rep. 100b; 2 Vent. 22; 14 East 64; Vaugh. 137; 1 Ashm. 140.'


4.  See Commonwealth v. Duane, 1806, reported in full in footnote (a) in Commonwealth v. Davies, 1804, 1 Bin. 97 and also see Commonwealth v. Keeper of the Prison, 1828, 1 Ashm. 140 discussed infra. More recently see Commonwealth v. McClain, 1936, 84 Pittsb.Leg.J. 171.


5.  This phrase from Magna Charta signifies that 'No free man shall be taken, or imprisoned, * * * except by legal judgment of his peers or the law of the land.'

 [***16] Counsel for the Commonwealth contended that the Dongagan case in 2 Yeates 437 foreclosed the question as to discretionary power to hold to bail for good behaviour, even after acquittal and recited a practice of doing so. Reference was made to 34 Edw. III, c. 1 for authority; that it was in force [***16] in this state: 3 Bin. 612; and that it gave discretionary power to a magistrate to take surety from all those whom he has just cause to suspect to be dangerous: 1 Hawk.P.C., Ch. 61, s. 4. The following is stated in the report of argument 10 Pa. at page 340:

'These are instances (i.e. the 'just cause') where defendants acquitted against plain evidence of felonies and other enormous crimes, have been bound to their good behavior: 2 Hawk.B. 2, ch. 47, s. 11; 2 Hale P.C. 136, in note, Am.edition, 1847. Here, the record shows the court below demanded surety on 'evidence presented' on a trial. Even at common law, a man might be bound to good behavior if it were testified he was of ill fame, and be committed till he finds sureties: 2 Hale P.C. 394. Surety for good behavior, even when defendant is not bound to answer, may be demanded: Com. v. Davies, 1 Bin. 98, [***17] note. The cases cited on the other side are cases of 'surety of the peace,' which differs from surety for good behavior, though the latter includes the former. 1 T.R. 696, was surety of the peace proper, and the point held was, that there might be a binding over for more than a year. 12 Ad. & Ellis, 599, was also surety of the [***17] peace. By the Act of 1700 (Purdon, 1083), previous oath in surety of peace is required; no such necessity in surety for good behavior. In Com. v. Keeper of the Prison, 1 Ashm. 140, it was held that an indefinite binding over to keep the peace and be of good behavior, could not be enforced in a matter of private wrong. Surety for good behavior includes surety for the peace, and something more. 4 Bl.Com. 256. A justice may bind over night-walkers, such as keep suspicious company, or are reported to be pilferers and robbers, and other persons 'whose misbehavior may reasonably bring them within the general words of the statute, as persons not of good fame; an expression, it must be owned, of so great latitude as leaves much to be determined by the discretion of the magistrate himself: 4 Bl.Com. 256, 257. A fortiori [***18] may a judge who, by 'evidence presented,' is satisfied that a man is not of good fame, and likely to endanger the public peace, demand surety for good behavior. Like all other discretionary powers, it should be used cautiously.'

It is apparent that the sole authorities relied upon by our cases in the Statute of 34 Edw. III, c. 1, and certain English cases and [***18] text writers which we shall discuss later. In the Report of the Judges of the Supreme Court as to which English Statutes are to be deemed in force in Pennsylvania 6 and which should be incorporated into the statute laws of this Commonwealth, 3 Bin. 595, et seq., at page 612; Roberts Digest of British Statutes, 349, it was indicated that 'those parts only of this statute are in force, which are distinguished by the numbers 2, 3, 4, 5, 6 and 10' and it was recommended that the statute be incorporated.


6.  Pursuant to Act of April 7, 1807, P.L. 163, 46 P.S. § 152 note.

The Statute of 34 Edw. III, c. 1, enacted in 1360, is as follows: 7


7.  We shall not here consider the interesting contention that this statute was incorrectly translated from the Norman French: See 27 Eng.Hist.Rev. 227 and see Lansbury v. Riley, (1914) 3 K.B. 229, 231.

('These be the things which our Lord, the King, the Prelates, Lords, and the Commons have ordained in this present parliament holden at Westminster, the Sunday next before the feast of the conversion of St. Paul, to be holden and published openly through the Realm.')

 [***19] Chapter 1. ('What sort of persons shall be Justices of Peace; and what [***19] authority they shall have.')

1. ('That in every County of England shall be assigned for the keeping of the peace, one Lord, and with him three or four of the most worthy in the County, with some learned in the law';)

2. 'And they shall have power to restrain the offenders, rioters, and all other barrators, and to pursue, arrest, take, and chastise them according to their trespass or offense';

3. 'And to cause them to be imprisoned and duly punished according to the law and customs of the Realm, and according to that which to them shall seem best to do by their discretion and good advertisement';

4. 'And also to inform them, and to inquire of all those that have been pillors and robbers in parts beyond the sea, and be now come again, and go wandering, and will not labor as they wont in time past';

5. 'And to take and arrest all those that they may find by indictment, or by suspicion, and to put them in prison';

6. 'And take of all them that be, not of good fame, where they shall be found, sufficient surety and mainprise of their good behavior towards the King and his People and the others [***20] duly to punish, to the intent that the people be not by such rioters or rebels [***20] troubled nor endangered, nor the peace blemished nor merchants nor others passing by the Highways of the realm disturbed, nor put in the peril which may happen of such offenders.'

7. ('And also to hear and determine at the King's suit all manner of felonies and trespass done in the same county according to the laws and customs aforesaid';)

8. ('And that Writs of Oyer and Determiner be granted according to the Statutes thereof made, and that the Justices which shall be thereto assigned be named by the Court, and not by the Party.')

9. ('And the King will, that all general inquiries before this Trial granted within any siginiories for the mischiefs and oppressions which have been done to the people by such inquiries, shall cease utterly and be repealed';)

10. 'And that fines, which are to be made before justices for a trespass done by any person, be reasonable and just, having regard to the quantity of the trespass and the cause for which they made.'

A reading of this statute nowhere indicates that a bond may be required of an accused following his trial and acquittal. Yet the [***21] power is traced to and said to be derived from paragraph 6 of this act which provided for the [***21] taking from 'them that be not of good fame * * * sufficient surtey and mainprise of their good behavior.' A study of the subject matter indicates that the so-called 'historical' basis for requiring the posting of a bond to keep the peace after acquittal is little more than bald assumption coupled with unsupported statements by legal writers based on cloudy old English cases whose very authority is left doubtful by subsequent English cases.

In 4 Blackstone 256 no reference is made to the specific question of whether a defendant may be required to post a bond for good behavior after acquittal, but reference over is made to 1 Hawkins, P.C. 132-133 where such authority is assumed. Blackstone first discusses the distinction between recognizances for the peace (what we refer to in this opinion as statutory peace bonds based on complaint and hearing) and for good behavior. He observes that a recognizance for good behavior 'includes security for the peace and somewhat more' and proceeds as follows:

'1. First, then, the justices are empowered, by the statute 34 Edw. III, c. 1, to [***22] bind over to the good behavior towards the king and his people all them that be not of good fame, wherever [***22] they be found; to the intent that the people be not troubled nor endamaged, nor the peace diminished, nor merchants and others, passing by the highways of the realm, be disturbed nor put in the peril which may happen by such offenders. Under the general words of this expression, that be not of good fame, it is holden that a man may be bound to his good behavior for causes of scandal, contra bonos mores, as well as contra pacem; as, for haunting bawdy-houses with women of bad fame, or for keeping such women in his own house, or for words tending to scandalize the government, or in abuse of the officers of justice, especially in the execution of their office. Thus also a justice may bind over all nightwalkers; eavesdroppers; such as keep suspicious company, or are reported to be pilferers or robbers; such as sleep in the day and wake in the night; common drunkards; whoremasters; the putative fathers of bastards; cheats; idle vagabonds; and other persons whose misbehavior may reasonably bring them within the general words of the statutes as persons not [***23] of good fame: an expression, it must be owned, of so great a latitude as to leave much to be determined by the discretion [***23] of the magistrate himself. But if he commits a man for want of sureties, he must express the cause thereof with convenient certainty, and take care that such cause be a good one.'

In 2 Hawkins, P.C., chap. 47, section 11, page 463 (8th Ed. 1824) Hawkins stated:

'That it has been adjudged, that if the jury acquitted a prisoner of an indictment of felony against manifest evidence, the court may, before the verdict is recorded, but not after, order them to go out again and reconsider the matter; but this is by me thought hard, and seems not of late years to have been so frequently practiced as formerly. Also there are instances where defendants acquitted against plain evidence, of felonies and other enormous crimes, have been bound to their good behavior.'

For this latter assertion, which is the point we are considering, Hawkins refers to three cases. 8 Hopestill Tyndal's Case, Cro.Car. 291, 79 Eng.Rep. 855 (1633); Evans and Cottington Case, Cro.Car. 507, 9 79 Eng.Rep. 1037 (1639); and 2 St.Tr. 60. The latter [***24] two cases we may well disregard since in both of them the power to punish was apparently based on the fact that the defendants were found guilty of contempt of court committed [***24] during the course of their respective trials. In the case of Evans and Cottington nine persons were indicted for grand riot and five of them were adjudged not guilty; as to three of them there was evidence of riot committed near the court. Hawkins indicates that the bond required was based on this contempt of court and this conclusion is strengthened by the fact that Justice Croke expressed no dissent as he did in the Hopestill Tyndal case.


8.  In 2 Hale's Pleas to the Crown 136-7 (Sun.Ed.1847) the subject matter is discussed but no additional judicial authority is referred to.


9.  Hawkins actually cites Cro.Jac. 507 but the cases there reported are obviously inappropriate and the reference must be as we have stated to the Evans case, which is cited by Hawkins in a footnote comment.

The only authority remaining upon which Hawkins relied is Rex v. Hopestill Tyndal's case, Cro.Car. 291, 79 Eng.Rep. 855 (1633), decided by an evenly divided court 10 of four Judges of the [***25] Court of King's Bench. The entire report of this case, together with the editor's footnotes, as it appears in the English Reprint is as follows:


10.  By familiar law this case being by an evenly divided court is not to be considered as binding precedent but adjudicates only the right of the parties in that particular case: Commonwealth ex rel. Vesneski v. Reid, 1919, 265 Pa. 328, 335, 108 A. 829; Griel's Estate, 1895, 171 Pa. 412, 416-417, 33 A. 375; Commonwealth v. Heller, 1942, 147 Pa.Super. 68, 78, 24 A.2d 460; Etting v. Bank of United States, 11 Wheat. 59, 78, 6 L.Ed. 419.

 [***25] 'The prisoner challenged one of the jurors, being the foreman, who was sworn, and marked sworn by the clerk, before the challenge was heard by the Court; and therefore without the assent of the Attorney General, then present, they would not alter the record; and because he would not assent to alter the record; the challenge was disallowed.

'And afterwards, upon evidence at the Bar, divers witnesses were produced by the defendant, which were heard without oath (1 Ann. c. 9 witnesses in behalf of a prisoner shall not be sworn. 2 Bulst. 147; [***26] 2 Hawk. c. 46, S. 29 and the cases there cited 4 Bl.Com. 353), but some of them witnessing matter which the Attorney General conceived would make for the King, whereupon the desire of the said attorney they were sworn, and after ordered upon their said oath to deliver their knowledge.'

'The prisoner was afterward acquitted, but because the evidence (if it had been believed by the jury) was strong against the prisoner, Richardson, Chief Justice and Jones, appointed, that the prisoner should be bound to his good behavior; whereupon, against the opinion of myself [Justice Croke] and Justice Berkley, he was so bound.'

Perhaps the most significant [***26] aspect of this opinion is that the statute of 34 Edward III, c. 1 is not cited as its authority; nor is there any reference to previous judicial precedents as there was on the point of taking the oath of witnesses.

In Rex v. Sir John Knight, Comberback 38, 39, 40, 90 Eng.Rep. 330, 331 (1687), cited in the Donagan case, the report lacks clarity and detail, but it would seem that the defendant was bound to good behavior after acquittal. The report indicates that an information was lodged 'for going to church with pistols,  [***27] etc. contra stat. 2 Ed. 3, * * *' and continued: 'C. J. This offence had been much greater, and better laid at common law. But tho' this statute be almost gone in desuetudinem, yet where the crime shall appear to be malo animo, it will come within the Act (tho' now there be a general connivance to gentlemen to ride armed for their security); but afterwards he was found, not guilty.'

Subsequently, and separated from the above report by the reports of several other cases, there appears at page 40 the following:

'One acquitted, yet bound to good behavior. Moved per Attorney-General, that the defendant, tho' found not guilty, may be bound to the good behavior, according [***27] to Jordan's case. And it was ordered accordingly per Cur.'

It is difficult to understand several aspects of this case. The separation of the two segments of the case is unexplained. It is not clear whether the proceeding involves a complaint by a third party or not; the procedure by information rather than by indictment is not explained. No report has been found of any pertinent 'Jordan's case' and we note that no reference is made to the statute of 34 Edward III, c. 1, though the prosecution was itself [***28] apparently based on an earlier statute of Edward III.

In reviewing texts and cases the following English cases are variously referred to as possible precedents for requiring a bond after acquittal: Rex v. Woolston, 2 Strange 834, 93 Eng.Rep. 881-2 (2 Geo. 2, 1729); Rex v. Martyn Page, Cro.Car. 332, 79 Eng.Rep. 890-1 (9 Car. 1, 1634); The King v. Andrew Robinson Bowes, 1 Term Rep. 696, 99 Eng.Rep. 1327-1330 (1787); Rex v. Holmes, Cro.Car. 378, 79 Eng.Rep. 928 (10 Car. 1, 1635). None of these cases is in point. 11


11.  See also The King v. Burford, 1 Ventris, 16, 86 Eng.Rep. 12 (21 Car. II, 1670) where defendant was indicted for scandalously stating that the justices did not understand the excise laws. It was held that the indictment should be quashed; that a man could not be indicted for speaking such words. The court then added that 'he might have been bound to his good behavior.' In Collin's v. Man, 1 Lev. 107, 83 Eng.Rep. 321 (15 Car. II, 1664) surety of peace ('bound to his good behavior') was required of defendant for using ill language in Westminster Hall.

 [***28] In the Woolston case defendant [***29] was convicted 'on four informations for blasphemous discourses on the miracles of our Saviour.' The court refused to entertain debate whether the offense was punishable at common law. The defendant was 'fined 25/ for each of his four discourses, to suffer a years imprisonment, and to enter into a recognizance for his good behavior during his life, himself in 3000/ and 2000/ by others.' There was no acquittal and the case is not pertinent to our inquiry.

In the Martyn Page case, the defendant was acquitted, by a jury, of rape upon a 10 year child because of lack of evidence of penetration and the judges who heard the evidence caused an indictment for battery to be preferred and found. Defendant was 'committed to prison, there to abide during the King's Pleasure, to be fined two hundred marks, to stand upon the pillory in Chancery-lane, in Middlesex, near the place where the fact was committed, with a paper upon his head signifying the cause, and to be bound with able sureties to the good behavior during life.' This case also did not involve the posting of security after acquittal.

In the Bowes case the court engaged in a lengthy discussion of [***29] the power of the court [***30] to require bail for longer than a year and in considerable sums but no issue of bond after acquittal was involved; the action was initiated on a complaint by the defendant's wife to have him enter security, a proceeding now governed by the Acts of 1860 and 1909 in Pennsylvania.

In contrast to the foregoing, reference may be made to Rex v. Holt, 7 Car. & P. 518, 524, 173 Eng.Rep. 229, 232 (1836) wherein Littledale, J., refused a request that he detain an acquitted defendant until articles of peace were prepared. Justice Littledale stated:

'When a verdict of Not guilty is found, and the Grand Jury are discharged, the prisoner is entitled to be discharged immediately.'

In Rex v. Dunn, 4 Per. & Dav. 437 (1840) after the defendant was discharged on articles of peace exhibited, the Attorney General moved that the defendant be bound for good behavior, Lord Denman, a distinguished common law judge, in denying that motion, stated (page 438):

'We think it must be taken that our judgment disposes of the whole matter up to the present time.'

On pages 434-435 Lord Denman states:

'The power of the justices of the peace in this behalf is traced to a statute of [***31] Edw.  [***30] 3, which was made the foundation of the commission of the peace, though some have thought that it did not warrant the crown in granting so large an authority. We cannot question the validity of the commission, which has been in operation for centuries; but the justices of the peace on the other hand cannot give themselves jurisdiction in any case. They cannot go beyond the terms and fair meaning of the commission. * * * The fair meaning is, that if one person informs the court or a justice of the peace that he goes in fear and in danger of personal violence from another, by reason of threats employed by him, and prays the protection of sureties of the peace, that protection may be granted. Unless such a case appear, no jurisdiction appears; nor can we ever infer facts necessary to give jurisdiction from the mere circumstance of an inferior court assuming to act as if they possessed it; least of all where, by the exercise of a discretionary power on an ex parte statement, which is not to be allowed to be contradicted, a single magistrate may deprive the subject of his personal freedom. An opportunity must therefore be given for arguing the validity of the act done [***32] by the sessions [***31] or magistrate, and unless this may be done by means of the writ of certiorari, it could never be done at all where the gaoler makes a general return to the habeas corpus.'

We have made extended reference to the cases in England and in this Commonwealth to show the dubious case basis on which there has been erected the present supposition that a defendant despite acquittal by a jury of his peers may nevertheless be bound over for his good behavior and, in default of the entry of the designated security, be forthwith imprisoned until such security be entered.

We turn now to a consideration of the petitioner's contention that the court's order and the statute which purportedly supplies its authority is in contravention of the petitioner's rights under the federal and the state constitutions. We are of the firm opinion that the statute is, on its face, under the decided cases and as administered, fatally defective because of vagueness; that because of such vagueness it contravenes Article 2, Section 1 of The Constitution of Pennsylvania as an improper delegation of legislative power to the judiciary. Further and concomitantly the guide 'not of good fame' which [***33] is the trigger mechanism [***32] for invocation of the statutory power to require a bond for good behavior is defectively vague and fails to provide any reasonably accurate ascertainable standard of guilt or to give reasonable notice of the conduct it purports to proscribe, contrary to the due process clause of the 14th amendment to the United States Constitution and contrary to the requirement of due process in Article 1, sections 1 and 9 of the constitution of this Commonwealth.

The Act of 34 Edw. III, c. 1, authorizes justices of the peace 12 to 'take of all them that be not of good fame * * * sufficient surety * * * of their good behavior * * * to the intent that the people be not by such rioters or rebels troubled nor endangered, nor the peace blemished nor merchants nor others passing by the Highways of the realm disturbed, nor put in the peril which may happen of such offenders.' The language of this section as well as that of the other sections quoted above is obviously addressed to a particular and peculiar situation then confronting the people of England in 1360. If we were to regard the statute as limited to the dangers engendered by 'rioters or rebels' it is obvious [***34] that the statute would be essentially [***33] inappropriate and of little consequence to the circumstances of our people. However, the significant words which have been judicially seized upon for the exercise of power in particular cases wherein judges have felt the juries went awry, are the words 'them that be not of good fame.' Is this a standard? We think not.


12.  We need not concern ourselves with the course by which judges of the courts of common pleas have acquired the powers granted to justices of the peace.

The Supreme Court of the United States has on frequent occasions considered the question of statutory vagueness. In Connally v. General Construction Co., 1925, 269 U.S. 385, 46 S.Ct. 126, 70 L.Ed. 322, the Supreme Court of the United States considered an Oklahoma statute, 61 O.S.1951 § 3, which imposed severe penalties upon contractors who paid their workmen less than the 'current rate of per diem wages in the locality where the work is performed * * *.' Mr. Justice Sutherland speaking for the court stated in 269 U.S. at pages 391-392, 46 S.Ct. at page 127:

'That the terms of a penal statute creating a new offense must be sufficiently [***35] explicit to inform those who are subject to it what conduct on their [***34] part will render them liable to its penalties is a well-recognized requirement, consonant alike with ordinary notions of fair play and the settled rules of law; and a statute which either forbids or requires the doing of an act in terms so vague that men of common intelligence must necessarily guess at its meaning and differ as to its application violates the first essential of due process of law. International Harvester Co. v. Kentucky, 234 U.S. 216, 221, 34 S.Ct. 853, 58 L.Ed. 1284; Collins v. Kentucky, 234 U.S. 634, 638, 34 S.Ct. 924, 58 L.Ed. 1510.

'The question whether given legislative enactments have been thus wanting in certainty has frequently been before this court. In some of the cases the statutes involved were upheld; in others, declared invalid. The precise point of differentiation in some instances is not easy of statement; but it will be enough for present purposes to say generally that the decisions of the court, upholding statutes as sufficiently certain, rested upon the conclusion that they employed words or phrases having a technical or other special meaning,  [***36] well enough known to enable those within their reach to correctly apply them, Hygrade Provision Co.  [***35] v. Sherman, 266 U.S. 497, 502, 45 S.Ct. 141, 69 L.Ed. 402; Omaechevarria v. Idaho, 246 U.S. 343, 348, 38 S.Ct. 323, 62 L.Ed. 763, or a well-settled common-law meaning, notwithstanding an element of degree in the definition as to which estimates might differ, Nash v. United States, 229 U.S. 373, 376, 33 S.Ct. 780, 57 L.Ed. 1232; International Harvester Co. v. Kentucky, supra, [234 U.S.] at page 223, 34 S.Ct. 853, or, as broadly stated by Mr. Chief Justice White in United States v. [L.] Cohen Grocery Co., 255 U.S. 81, 92, 41 S.Ct. 298, 301, 65 L.Ed. 516, 'that, for reasons found to result either from the text of the statutes involved or the subjects with which they dealt, a standard of some sort was afforded.' See also, Waters-Pierce Oil Co. v. Texas (No. 1), 212 U.S. 86, 108, 29 S.Ct. 220, 53 L.Ed. 417. Illustrative cases on the other hand are International Harvester Co. v. Kentucky, supra, Collins v. Kentucky, supra, and United States v. [L.] Cohen Grocery Co., supra, [***37] and cases there cited. The Cohen Grocery case involved the validity of section 4 of the Food Control Act of 1917, which imposed a penalty upon any person who should make 'any unjust or unreasonable rate or charge,  [***36] in handling or dealing in or with any necessaries.' It was held that these words fixed no ascertainable standard of guilt, in that they forbade no specific or definite act.'

The Court quoted with approval in 269 U.S. at pages 392-393, 46 S.Ct. at page 128 the following from United States v. Capital Traction Co., 1910, 34 App.D.C. 592, 596, 598:

'The statute makes it a criminal offense for the street railway companies in the District of Columbia to run an insufficient number of cars to accommodate persons desiring passage thereon, without crowding the same. What shall be the guide to the court or jury in ascertaining what constitutes a crowded car? What may be regarded as a crowded car by one jury may not be so considered by another. What shall constitute a sufficient number of cars in the opinion of one judge may be regarded as insufficient by another. * * * There is a total absence of any definition of what shall constitute a crowded [***38] car. This important element cannot be left to conjecture, or be supplied by either the court or the jury. It is of the very essence of the law itself, and without it the statute is too indefinite and uncertain to support an information or indictment.

 [***37] '* * * The dividing line between what is lawful and unlawful cannot be left to conjecture. The citizen cannot be held to answer charges based upon penal statutes whose mandates are so uncertain that they will reasonably admit of different constructions. A criminal statute cannot rest upon an uncertain foundation. The crime, and the elements constituting it, must be so clearly expressed that the ordinary person can intelligently choose, in advance, what course it is lawful for him to pursue. Penal statutes prohibiting the doing of certain things, and providing a punishment for their violation, should not admit of such a double meaning that the citizen may act upon the one conception of its requirements and the courts upon another.'

The opinion of the court concluded 269 U.S. at page 395, 46 S.Ct. at page 129:

'The result is that the application of the law depends, not upon a word of fixed meaning in itself,  [***39] or one made definite by statutory or judicial definition, or by the context or other legitimate aid to its construction, but upon the probably varying impressions of juries as to whether given areas are or are not to be included within particular localities. The constitutional [***38] guaranty of due process cannot be allowed to rest upon a support so equivocal.'

In Lanzetta v. New Jersey, 1938, 306 U.S. 451, 59 S.Ct. 618, 83 L.Ed. 888, there was under consideration a statute which provided, 'Any person not engaged in any lawful occupation, known to be a member of any gang consisting of two or more persons, who has been convicted at least three times of being a disorderly person, or who has been convicted of any crime * * * is declared to be a gangster * * *' and imposed severe penalties. The court discussed at length the word 'gang' employed in the statute and stated that it had no 'meaning derivable from the common law, for neither in that field nor anywhere in the language of the law is there definition of the word' 306 U.S. at page 455, 59 S.Ct. at page 621 and concluded 'The challenged provision condemns no act or omission; the terms it employs to [***40] indicate what it purports to denounce are so vague, indefinite and uncertain that it must be condemned as repugnant to the due process clause of the Fourteenth Amendment.' See Champlin Rfg. Co. v. Corp. Commission, 1932, 286 U.S. 210, 242, 243, 52 S.Ct. 559, 76 L.Ed. 1062; Cline v. Frink Dairy Co., 1927, 274 U.S.  [***39] 445, 458, 47 S.Ct. 681, 71 L.Ed. 1146; Small Co. v. American Sugar Rfg. Co., 1925, 267 U.S. 233, 239, 45 S.Ct. 295, 69 L.Ed. 589; Nash v. United States, 1913, 229 U.S. 373, 33 S.Ct. 780, 57 L.Ed. 1232. See the discussions in Winters v. New York, 1948, 333 U.S. 507, 68 S.Ct. 665, 92 L.Ed. 840; Jordan v. DeGeorge, 1951, 341 U.S. 223, 71 S.Ct. 703, 95 L.Ed. 886, especially Justice Jackson's dissenting opinion joined in by Mr. Justice Black and Mr. Justice Frankfurter; Edmond N. Cahn, Authority and Responsibility, 51 Col.L.R. 838 (Nov. 1951); Musser v. Utah, 1948, 333 U.S. 95, 68 S.Ct. 397, 92 L.Ed. 562; People v. Belcastro, 1934, 356 Ill. 144, 190 N.E. 301, 92 A.L.R. 1223; People v. Licavoli, 1933, 264 Mich. 643, 250 N.W. 520; Ralph W. Sigler,  [***41] Legislation in Vague or General Terms, 21 Mich.L.Rev. 831 (1923); Vernon L. Wilkinson, The Federal Bill of Rights and Fourteenth Amendment, 26 Geo.L.J. 439 (1938).

In State v. Partlow, 1884, 91 N.C. 550, 553, Judge Merrimon stated, 'But a statute must be capable of construction and interpretation; otherwise it will be inoperative and void. The court must use every authorized means to ascertain and give it an intelligible meaning, but if after [***40] such effort it is found to be impossible to solve the doubt and dispel the obscurity, if no judicial certainty can be settled upon as to the meaning, the court is not at liberty to supply, to make one. The court may not allow 'conjectural interpretation to usurp the place of judicial exposition.' There must be a competent and efficient expression of the legislative will. In Drake v. Drake, 1833, [15 N.C. 110] Chief Justice Ruffin said, 'Whether a statute be a public or a private one, if the terms in which it is couched be so vague as to convey no definite meaning to those whose duty it is to execute it, either ministerially or judicially, it is necessarily inoperative. The law [***42] must remain as it was, unless that which professes to change it be itself intelligible.'

In Stoutenburgh v. Frazier, 1900, 16 App.D.C. 299, 48 L.R.A. 220, the court considered a statute in which the language was similar to that used in the statute of 34 Edward III, c. 1. Congress enacted a statute relative to suspicious persons and vagrants which read in part as follows:

'That all vagrants; all idle and disorderly persons; persons of evil life or evil fame * * * shall, upon conviction thereof, be [***41] fined not to exceed forty dollars, or required to enter into security for good behavior for a period of six months.' (Emphasis added.)

The defendant was arrested, tried and convicted on a charge of being a suspicious person. The court stated

'Suspicion may exist without even the knowledge of the party who is the object of the suspicion. The suspicion may be generated in the mind of one or more persons without even colorable foundation of truth for the suspicion; yet the party, the object of the suspicion, may, under the statute * * * be seized, imprisoned, tried and convicted merely because some persons or police officer may have concluded (whether upon reasonable [***43] grounds or not) that he was a suspicious person. Of what suspected and what degree of suspicion exists, must always be the first thought that occurs upon such a charge as that made in this case. But here the party is charged, in an abstract way, with being a suspicious person merely, there being no act or conduct of his, mentioned in the statute, to which the suspicion could relate. How is he to meet such a charge? * * * A suspicious character, however, does not constitute crime, nor does it justify the government [***42] in treating the party from having such reputation as criminal, without connecting him with some criminal act or conduct.'

The Court then proceeded to rule the statute to be unconstitutional, in conflict with the federal constitutional prohibitions against unreasonable searches and seizures and cruel and unusual punishments and in the course of its opinion pertinently stated:

'In the legislation of England, in regard to the police of that Kingdom, we find a great variety of regulations prescribed from time to time, dating back to the earlier reigns and all those in succession; down to modern times, to meet the requirements of the then existing state of society.  [***44] But much of that legislation has become obsolete, and, from its severe restrictions upon the liberties of the people, and the cruel and unnatural punishments inflicted, it has long since ceased to be applicable to modern society, and could not at this day be tolerated. The history of that legislation is clearly stated by Mr. Justice Stephen in the Third Volume of his History of the Criminal Law of England, chap. 32, pp. 263-274, and in 2 Broom & Hadley, Com. 467.'
