See also People v. Alterie, 1934, 356 Ill. 307, 190 N.E.  [***43] 305; State v. Kartz, 1882, 13 R.I. 528; State v. Beswick, 1881, 13 R.I. 211; Greene v. Briggs, Fed.Cas.No. 5,764, 1 Curt. 311; Huntsman v. State, 1882, 12 Tex.App. 619 for consideration of punishment because of reputation.

Applying then the principles of these cases to the statute of 34 Edw. III we cannot find any standard known to the law by which to measure the exercise of judicial power it purports to create. The phrase 'not of good fame' is a sheer reference to general reputation; in its broadest sense it encompasses the entire expanse of conceived contemporaneous morality. It admits [***45] of any definition according to the experience and opinion of the definer. In the Lanzetta case, supra, the Supreme Court of the United States stated 306 U.S. at page 453, 59 S.Ct. at page 619:

'If on its face the challenged provision is repugnant to the due process clause, specification of details of the offense intended to be charged would not serve to validate it. Cf. United States v. Reese, 92 U.S. 214, 221, 23 L.Ed. 563; Czarra v. Board of Medical Supervisors, 25 App.D.C. 443, 453. It is the statute, not the accusation under it, that prescribes the rule to govern conduct and warns against [***44] transgression. See Stromberg v. California, 283 U.S. 359, 368, 51 S.Ct. 532, 535, 75 L.Ed. 1117; Lovell v. Griffin, 303 U.S. 444, 58 S.Ct. 666, 82 L.Ed. 949. No one may be required at peril of life, liberty or property to speculate as to the meaning of penal statutes. All are entitled to be informed as to what the State commands or forbids.' [Citing, by footnote, numerous cases.]

We have examined every pertinent case, modern and ancient, which we could find or to which we were referred and we are unable to conclude [***46] that the phrase 'not of good fame' has in any way been made definite by judicial construction or definition and it is certainly not made definite by context or by the evil it was designed to cure: see Connally v. General Const. Co., supra, 269 U.S. at page 395, 46 S.Ct. 126. Commonwealth v. Foster, 1905, 28 Pa.Super. 400 is the only case we have found which actually considered the purport and scope of the words 'not of good fame.' In that case the defendant had been convicted several times 'of doing worldly business on Sunday' in violation of the Act of April 22, 1794, 3 Sm.L. 177. Upon complaint by affidavit the court of quarter sessions required of him security [***45] to be of good behavior. The Superior Court reversed this order. Mr. Justice Potter, on behalf of the court, concluded that the defendant was not the type of offender contemplated by the Act of 34 Edward III, c. 1. There is language in this opinion which reflects the assumption that there exists a power to bind over after acquittal in a 'proper' case, but the cases and authorities relied upon are those we have already made reference to. This case has not cured the vagueness of the [***47] statute. Perhaps the most striking aspect of the judicial history of the language in the statute 34 Edw. III, c. 1, is that in 592 years it has received no definition other than in a few isolated negative instances. The illustrations used by Blackstone, Hawkins and Burn are either obviously inappropriate or so broad as to be as meaningless as the phrase to be defined. It is also obvious that many of these illustrations could only apply after a conviction.

Assuming, however, that the power to require bond for good behavior is somehow limited by the words 'not of good fame' as a purported reference to our criminal law or criminal prosecutions, to just what criminal acts does it have reference?  [***46] Does it refer to previous convictions? The suggestion that any person who has suffered a previous conviction is subject to being bound to his good behavior if he is again accused and acquitted is to suggest a second punishment for the same offense. Is the conclusion to be based upon evidence which we must assume the jury disbelieved? If so, evidence of what? Mr. Justice Potter in the Foster case stated, 'This record contains nothing from which it could be inferred [***48] that there was any reasonable ground to apprehend or that this justice of the peace apprehended, that this defendant would commit any felony or misdemeanor or disturb the peace, or in any way trouble, disturb or injure any of the people of the state, in their persons or property.' Honest observation of the practice in, at least, the courts of this county would compel admission that few records reflect the matters referred to by Judge Potter. The real function of the practice is to substitute a judicial finding of guilty for the jury's acquittal; it is punishment for past iniquity; especially is this so where the bond is fixed so high that the defendant (recently freed by the verdict) is committed for default. 13 [***47] It substitutes suspicion of what a man may do for proof of violation of the criminal laws. This without a trial of the suspicion before a jury. Surely this is not due process of law.


13.  In the Pennsylvania Report of Legislative Commission to Investigate Administration of Criminal Justice (July, 1938), it was stated, 'A practice has grown up among certain judges in Philadelphia of holding in 'Bonds to keep the Peace' defendants who have been acquitted by juries, and in some cases, persons who have not been charged with any criminal offense. The bonds are often fixed at an exorbitant sum which such persons cannot procure and the effect is to keep men in prison for long periods of time until the judge decides to release them.' The commission also stated, '* * * it is the conclusion of many eminent jurists, lawyers and scholars, that such procedure is clearly unconstitutional and a flagrant and dangerous abuse of judicial power.' It is clear, however, that the Commission did not rely upon any judicial authorities for its assertion and we are now to determine whether that expressed opinion was correct.

 [***49] Closely related to the problem of vagueness as an infringement on due process [***48] is the problem of the delegation of legislative power to the judiciary. It is particularly pertinent in the matter we are considering because the legislation purports to vest power in the hands of the judiciary as an aspect of their ancient role of conservators of the peace.

By Article II, section 1 of the Pennsylvania Constitution all legislative power is vested exclusively in the General Assembly. In Kellerman v. Philadelphia, 1939, 139 Pa.Super. 569, 575, 13 A.2d 84, 86, the principle was enunciated that 'any legislative enactment which vests in a person or body of persons free of any standard independent of his or their own mind and judgment the power of supplying, or giving force to, or suspending its terms * * *' is unconstitutional. See O'Neil v. American Fire Ins. Co., 1895, 166 Pa. 72, 30 A. 943, 26 L.R.A. 715; Bell Telephone Co. of Penn. v. Driscoll, 1941, 343 Pa. 109, 21 A.2d 912; Holgate Bros. Co. v. Bashore, 1938, 331 Pa. 255, 200 A. 672, 117 A.L.R. 639; American Baseball Club of Philadelphia v. Philadelphia, 1933, 312 Pa. 311, 167 A. 891, 92 A.L.R. 386; [***50] Wilson v. Philadelphia School Dist., 1937, 328 Pa. 225, 195 A. 90, 113 A.L.R. 1401. This rule applies as well to [***49] prohibit the vesting of legislative power in the judiciary: see State ex rel. Patterson v. Bates, 1905, 96 Minn. 110, 104 N.W. 709; Burnett v. Greene, 1929, 97 Fla. 1007, 122 So. 570, 69 A.L.R. 244; In re Opinion of Justices, 1932, 279 Mass. 607, 180 N.E. 725, 81 A.L.R. 1059; Searle v. Yensen, 1929, 118 Neb. 835, 226 N.W. 464, 69 A.L.R. 257.

Undoubtedly the principle of the separation of powers in the three departments of government applies with least force and effect to the judiciary insofar as the entire concept of judicial discretion has been evolved. But it has never been supposed that a legislature may under the guise of reliance upon discretion abdicate its power and transfer it to the judiciary. When courts are said to exercise discretion the reference is to legal discretion within not without the bounds of the law. The rule was most aptly phrased by Chief Justice Marshall in Osborn v. Bank of U. S., 1824, 9 Wheat. 738, 866, 6 L.Ed. 204:

'Judicial power, as contradistinguished [***51] from the power of the laws, has no existence. Courts are the mere instruments of the law, and can will nothing. When they are said to exercise a discretion, it is a mere legal discretion,  [***50] a discretion to be exercised in discerning the course prescribed by law, and when that is discerned, it is the duty of the court to follow it. Judicial power is never exercised for the purpose of giving effect to the will of the judge; always for the purpose of giving effect to the will of the Legislature; or in other words, to the will of the law.' (emphasis added). See Maloney v. Stahlnecker, 1941, 341 Pa. 517, 522, 19 A.2d 162; Dauphin County Grand Jury Investigative Proceedings No. 3, 1938, 332 Pa. 358, 364, 2 A.2d 809; Schlaudecker v. Marshall, 1872, 72 Pa. 200, 206; Melnick v. Melnick, 1942, 147 Pa.Super. 564, 570, 25 A.2d 111.

The purport of the foregoing cases and principles must be that the legislature has no right to confer or the judiciary to accept a power in terms so broad and meaning so vague that the application or non-application of the law depends wholly upon the individual opinion and predilections of the trial [***52] judge. Any statute which makes the judge's discretion the only rule for his conduct must be regarded as invalid: see King v. State, 1889, 87 Tenn. 304, 10 S.W. 509, 3 L.R.A. 210.

What guide is there in the language of 34 Edw. III whereby the exercise [***51] of judicial discretion is to be tested. We have already set forth the aspects of vagueness in the statute whereby it fails to meet the minimum requirements of due process and we need not repeat ourselves. It is sufficient to note that nowhere is there an intelligible standard whereby a judge may consider the propriety of exercising his discretion in regard to whatever facts may appear; the words 'not of good fame' receive no clarification in the early English cases prior to the formation of this Commonwealth nor in the works of ancient and respected textwriters. The words 'not of good fame' do not have any settled reference to any defined act or series of acts; there need not be proved any criminal intent or purpose; there is no hearing on the issue of 'good fame' nor is there any necessity to indicate the facts found or the reasons for the judge's conclusion. Indeed, what we do find in the old cases is a singular [***53] awareness of the danger in the virtually untrammeled power apparently claimed. See the quotation from Blackstone, supra, 5 Burn's Just. 746.

In Commonwealth v. Davies, 1804, 1 Bin. 97 there appears in a footnote the opinion of Chief Justice Tilghman in the case of Commonwealth [***52] v. Duane, decided on August 11, 1806. In that case, upon complaint of certain parties through the attorney general as to the publication of past libels, the Mayor of Philadelphia directed Duane to enter into a recognizance for appearance at the next Mayor's court and enter security for good behavior until his appearance at such court. Apparently he was committed upon failure to post security for good behavior. In a habeas corpus proceeding it was held that he need only post security for appearance at court and not for good behavior. Chief Justice Tilghman 14 discussed the matter at length and stated 1 Bin. at pages 98, 99:


14.  When it is considered that Justice Tilghman was Chief Justice of the Supreme Court when the Report of the Judges was made as to English Statutes in force in this commonwealth his expressed view that surety for good behavior could be demanded either after conviction as part of the judgment of the court or 'before the trial' of a 'person charged with an offense' is entitled to careful consideration in determining the extent of the authority conferred by 34 Edward III., c. 1.

 [***54] 'Surety for good behavior may be considered in two points of view. It [***53] is either required after conviction of some indictable offence, in which case it forms part of the judgment of the court, and is founded on a power incident to courts of record by the common law, or it is demanded by judges or justices of the peace out of court, before the trial of the person charged with an offence, in pursuance of authority derived from a statute made in the 34th year of Edward 3. It is this last kind of surety we are now to consider. The statute 34 Edward 3, authorizes justices of the peace to take surety for good behavior of all those that are not of good fame, to the intent that the public may not be troubled by such persons. It is supposed that this statute was made to prevent the disorders which were introduced by the soldiers of Edward the third, numbers of whom, after serving in his armies in France, were discharged in England. The natural meaning of the words 'persons not of good fame' seems to be, those who by their general evil course and habits of life had acquired a bad reputation, and were supposed to be dangerous to the community. In process of time, however, the construction [***55] of these expressions has been extended far beyond their original meaning,  [***54] and persons are now commonly held to find surety for their good behavior, who are not generally of ill fame, but have only been charged with some particular offence. It is laid down by some ancient authors, that libellers may be held to surety for good behavior. * * *'

There then followed a discussion of surety for good behavior to prevent libel and of the contention that it was a thing 'of course.' The Chief Justice continued 1 Bin. at page 99:

'* * * Now if this practice is established, two consequences will follow, which certainly may be attended with great inconvenience. In the first place the Justice who takes the recognizance may fix it in whatever sum he pleases, and then if it should be forfeited by a libel of the mildest nature, the whole penalty must be recovered, without any power in the court to mitigate the punishment according to the nature of the offence. And in the second place, the defendant may be brought to trial for a libel, so far as to be burthened with the forfeiture of his recognizance, without the previous investigation of a grand jury. No considerate man [***56] will say that under certain circumstances these may not be very great evils. No man can exactly calculate [***55] how far a practice of this kind, exercised by wicked and daring hands, into which it may sometimes fall, may stifle or even extinguish the spirit of honest investigation and necessary inquiry. And what is the occasion for it? The party complaining has a right to the protection of the laws and will receive it. The person accused will be brought to his trial, and if convicted he will be punished accordingly to the degree of the offence. What more does public justice require? But it is said, it is necessary to prevent future libels. If future libels are published while the prosecution is depending, they will be punished on conviction in proportion to the obstinacy of the offender. No man abhors more than I do the base practice of libelling. It is a crime forbidden by the laws of God and man, and of a much blacker dye than many men seem to be aware of. All classes and descriptions of men, all parties have in their turn lamented and suffered by the uncontrolled licentiousness of the press. I am not without hopes that the evil will be lessened, that a remedy will [***57] be found in the honesty and good sense of a majority of the people, aided by the wholesome chastisement which courts [***56] and injuries will be called on from time to time to inflict. But in order to give those punishments their full efficacy in the community, it will be necessary to judicial proceedings to temper firmness with liberality, never forgetting that humane principle, which in doubtful cases turns the scale in favor of the accused.

'* * * In the present case, Duane is charged with publishing two libels against the same person, and he has not confessed that he is the author of either. As a judge, I know nothing that is not legally proved before me. I must not act on the supposition that the defendant has published numerous libels, because there is no oath to that purpose, and by our constitution all warrants must be ground upon an oath or affirmation. Upon the whole, the most that can be said with regard to recognizances for good behavior is, that they are demandable or not, at the discretion of the judge. They differ from recognizances to keep the peace, in two important features: 1. Surety for good behavior is more extensive in its nature than surety for the peace,  [***58] and may be more easily forfeited, and therefore should be exacted with greater caution. 2. Surety of the peace is demandable [***57] of right by any individual who thinks himself in danger of bodily hurt, and will make the necessary oaths; but this principle has not been applied to surety for good behavior. I will not say that there are no circumstances in which surety for good behavior ought not to be exacted in cases of libels before conviction; on the contrary, I have no doubt but there are occasions on which it may be proper and necessary to insist on it. But I am of opinion that it will be most agreeable to the spirit of our constitution, and most conducive to the suppression of libels, to adopt it as a rule, not to demand surety for good behavior before conviction.'

In Commonwealth v. Keeper of the Prison, (1828?), 1 Ashm. 140, a habeas corpus proceeding, the relators had been 'required to give bail for future good conduct and behavior towards 'a named individual' as well as to all the good citizens of the commonwealth'; upon refusal to enter bail they were committed until the 'next Mayor's Court.' The court in its opinion discussed the facts at length indicating [***59] that there was an issue as to whether defendants had committed a forcible entry. The question was as to the propriety of obtaining surety of [***58] the peace against future repetition; the relator had never been tried. President Judge King stated in 1 Ashm. at page 148 that guilt should be 'as clear as the sun in the firmament' to

'authorize a judge to usurp the functions of a court and jury, and to decide, first that a crime accompanied with a breach of peace has been committed, and that the party charged should be coerced under pain of imprisonment to give surety against its future repetition. * * * The relators before I should demand surety of the peace from them on the assumption that they are forcible enterers and rioters, have the legal and constitutional right of having that vital question determined by a jury of their peers. The argument that a demand of surety of the peace is a more mild and merciful proceeding than a prosecution by indictment, is more specious than solid. In effect it gives to a single magistrate, a power which pertains only to a court and jury, and in substance deprives the accused of what is his most inestimable right, the trial by [***60] jury. I would ask the advocates of this doctrine what is to limit the magistrate or subsequently the court to which suretyships are returnable, either as to the length [***59] of time for which they shall be continued, or the amount of bail by which they shall be rendered effectual. Moderate bail and the party's recognizance is only demanded in this instance by a mild and humane magistrate, but under less auspicious circumstances a different requisition might be made, and the citizen deprived of his liberty without 'the judgment of his peers,' or as I believe 'the law of the land.' This doctrine is not novel. 'Surety of the peace,' says Dalton, page 266, 'ought not to be granted on account of a past beating, unless there be fear of future danger: the remedy in such case being by action or indictment.' How much stronger is this case where the evidence renders it doubtful whether a public offence, a private injury, or any wrong has been done, from that put by this ancient author, where the act is necessarily criminal, and the threat to repeat it, a threat to commit a certain crime.

'The Mayor's Court of the city is now in session and the grand jury not discharged. If it is desired [***61] to have a judicial decision on the character of this entry, let a bill of indictment be submitted to that body. By certiorari such a bill would be removed to the Supreme Court,  [***60] and the judgment of that dignified tribunal between these parties ascertained. If Jeremiah Willetts feels himself personally aggrieved by Edmund Shotwell, the Courts of the Commonwealth are open to him. But until the guilt of these defendants of the crimes imputed to them is duly substantiated, I cannot under the circumstances of this case, demand of them a surety which can never be justly called for on the mere assumption of their guilt. On the whole I am compelled to differ from what I have no doubt was the sincere and conscientious judgment of the Mayor, and accordingly refuse to call on the relators to give surety to keep the peace. No other requisition being made of me, they are discharged and at liberty to go where they please.'

Not only do we find that there is no limitation set forth as to when the trial judge shall exercise his 'discretion' requiring bond for good behavior, but as pointed out in the three preceding cases there is no limitation as to the period of time during which [***62] bond shall be required or as to the amount of the bond to be required. The prohibition against unrestrained power to define the substance of penal conditions is also a prohibition against [***61] unrestrained power to determine the sanctions which are to be applied. In South Bethlehem v. Hackett, 1895, 4 North. Co. 381, 12 Lanc.L.R. 196, it was held that a statute which made failure to pay a certain license tax a crime punishable by a fine of not less than $ 100 was invalid as an improper delegation of legislative power because it did not fix a limit to the amount of the fines which could be imposed. 15


15.  Compare the Act of May 27, 1919, P.L. 306, 19 P.S. §§ 21, 22, empowering Justices of the Peace, in the exercise of discretion, to impose costs on the defendant, despite acquittal. In Commonwealth ex rel. Heydt v. Bossler, 1919, 29 Pa.Dist.R. 171, this practice was ruled invalid. See also Commonwealth v. Webster, 1924, 23 Luz.Leg.Reg. 359; Commonwealth v. Cooper, 1921, 21 Luz.Leg.Reg. 264; Commonwealth v. Emerick, 1924, 6 Pa.Dist. & Co. 434; Commonwealth v. Reynolds, 1925, 26 Lack.Jur. 133; contra Commonwealth v. Brown, 1922, 1 Pa.Dist. & Co. 609. In the Webster case, supra, President Judge Fuller stated, 'We feel impelled to add that this whole matter of costs under our system of criminal jurisprudence is an instrument of oppressive cruelty which belongs to the Dark Ages, and should not be tolerated in an age which claims to be civilized.'

 [***63]  [***62] From our consideration of this matter we are of the opinion that the only possible basis for conceding contemporary validity to this 1360 statute is its age and the age of certain ill-considered text comments, a variation of the supposition that 'whatever is is right.' We choose, however, to conclude that civil rights are not lost by historical default; that error compounded by error remains nonetheless error. Age alone certainly does not render a statute invalid; nor does the fact that it was adopted in and for different times and purposes. Conversely, however, age alone cannot insulate a statute from attack when in different time and social context and in different constitutional milieu it is in irreconcilable conflict with concepts of individual rights and criminal law.

Counsel for the petitioner in his excellent brief has indicated at length the vast and significant differences in the world of 1360 and the world of 1952. We shall not undertake any extended historical review of those differences. Suffice it to note that that statute fell into a period of disorganization, riot, illness and death. In Commonwealth v. Duane, supra, Chief Justice Tilghman correctly [***64] observed that [***63] the reason for the statute was that, due to temporary peace, the soldiers of Edward III returned from years of plundering and pillaging in France, which practice they apparently continued in England. The condition of Europe and England is set forth in Volume IV (Vol. 1 of the series, The State of Europe during the Middle Ages) of the monumental work of Henry Hallam (10th ed., Little Brown & Company, Boston 1853) in chapter I, part II. For one hundred and twenty years war between France and England raged in greater magnitude than any since the fall of the Roman Empire, interrupted once by a truce in 1357 by the Peace of Bretigni. During the same period pestilence laid waste the peoples of Europe and England and Hallam indicates what must have 'returned home' to England in a passage as follows (pages 56-57):

'There is no affliction which did not fall upon France during this miserable period. A foreign enemy was in the heart of the kingdom, the king was a prisoner, the capital in sedition, * * * Famine, the sure and terrible companion of war, for several years desolated the country. In 1348, a pestilence, the most extensive and unsparing of which we have [***65] any memorial, visited France [***64] as well as the rest of Europe, and consummated the work of hunger and the sword. The companies of adventure, mercenary troops in the service of John or Edward, finding no immediate occupation after the truce of 1357, scattered themselves over the country, in search of pillage. No force existed sufficiently powerful to check these robbers in their career.'

See L. Owen Pike, 1 History of Crime in England, chapter 4 (1873); Bertha H. Putnam, The Ames Foundation, Proceedings Before the Justices of the Peace, Edward 3rd to Richard 3rd, page 33 (1938); Sir James F. Stephen, A History of the Criminal Law of England, London (1883); 2 Gabbett, Criminal Law 121 (1843).

In final analysis the power which the judiciary has arrogated unto it itself is a crystalization of a judicial attitude that the jury is not to be fully and finally trusted with its function. The power to bind to good behavior after acquittal is and has been used when the trial judge is of the opinion that the jury erred in its verdict. Without discussing the nature, history and function of the jury, in an already over-extended opinion, it should be sufficient to observe that the jury [***66] is more than just a juridical instrument,  [***65] it is a political institution which should not be hastily infringed upon in particular and difficult cases lest the generality of freedom which it purports to represent be undermined without planned substitute. To the jury belongs the power to err, not alone in gullibility but also deliberately. A most important portion of the administration of our system of criminal justice is the fact that the jury in subtle ways may temper the rigidity of our criminal code in the application of the letter of the law to particular cases and may perhaps thereby mitigate the rigors of the law. 16


16.  Consider, for example, the manner in which eighteenth century juries informally amended the broad scope of the law's definition of capital offenses by simply refusing to return guilty verdicts in cases in which such penalty offended the conscience of the people. Also, recall the early trials of Peter Zenger and William Penn.

Finally we may state of record that the essentially real basis for our decision is simply that we consider the practice under review to be wrong. We are of the firm conviction that the practice is fundamentally [***67] in conflict with any modern and enlightened view of [***66] individual civil rights; that it offends the spirit and instinct, and the very letter of due process. The practice of binding after acquittal, even if we were to assume a proper historical basis for it, which we do not, is an anachronism; it is a vestige of a social form which has passed and it cannot coexist with the modern concept of due process; it has been repealed by change. Our very jurisprudence is predicated upon rule by law as distinguished from rule by man. The evil of the practice we are considering is that it is in reality an effective power to punish in virtually unrestrained form. Suppositions of right to review are in practice unrealistic. The liberties of the people are not to be committed to such despotic power -- be it executive, legislative or judicial; nor are these liberties to be diluted by pretense and supposition that such virtually unreviewable power will be wielded sparingly.

Regardless of the state of the law prior to the 14th amendment we hold that the modern concept of the process has, in any event, rendered obsolete the practice of requiring security after acquittal. That change and [***68] growth are implicit in the concept of due process was indicated [***67] by Mr. Justice Frankfurter in Wolf v. Colorado, 1947, 338 U.S. 25, 27, 69 S.Ct. 1359, 1361, 93 L.Ed. 1782:

'Due process of law thus conveys neither formal nor fixed nor narrow requirements. It is the compendious expression for all those rights which the courts must enforce because they are basic to our free society. But basic rights do not become petrified as of any one time, even though, as a matter of human experience, some may not too rhetorically be called eternal verities. It is of the very nature of a free society to advance in its standards of what is deemed reasonable and right. Representing as it does a living principle, due process is not confined within a permanent catalogue of what may at a given time be deemed the limits or the essentials of fundamental rights.

'To rely on a tidy formula for the easy determination of what is a fundamental right for purposes of legal enforcement may satisfy a longing for certainty but ignores the movements of a free society. It belittles the scale of the conception of due process. The real clue to the problem confronting the judiciary in the application [***69] of the Due Process Clause is not to ask where the line is once and for all to [***68] be drawn but to recognize that it is for the Court to draw it by the gradual and empiric process of 'inclusion and exclusion.' Davidson v. New Orleans, 96 U.S. 97, 104, 24 L.Ed. 616. This was the Court's insight when first called upon to consider the problem; to this insight the Court has on the whole been faithful as case after case has come before it since Davidson v. New Orleans was decided.'

The practice we have reviewed is in reality not in any real sense a method of 'preventive justice.' When a person is committed for default of entry of security after acquittal he is simply being punished without trial. If this power so clearly derives from the common law, why does it not exist in a single one of our sister states?

Order

And Now, July 15, 1952, the rule heretofore granted upon the District Attorney to show cause why the recognizance required of petitioner should not be quashed and discharged is made absolute.

 

690 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth ex rel. Sholter v. Claudy


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


171 Pa. Super. 442; 90 A.2d 343; 1952 Pa. Super. LEXIS 373


April 16, 1952, Argued  
July 17, 1952 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal, No. 83, Oct. T., 1952, from order of Court of Common Pleas of Centre County, Dec. T., 1951, No. 165, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania ex rel. George F. Sholter v. Dr. John W. Claudy, Warden, Western State Penitentiary.

Habeas corpus proceeding. Before Walker, P.J.

Order entered dismissing rule to show cause and refusing writ. Relator appealed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Centre County (Pennsylvania), which dismissed his petition for a writ of habeas corpus.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was sentenced to serve a term for a conviction on three charges of robbery and attempted robbery. He was transferred to a prison farm from which he escaped. He was then apprehended in another state, returned, and placed in jail. He entered a plea of guilty to the crime of prison breach. He unsuccessfully filed a first petition for a writ of habeas corpus. His second petition was also dismissed. On appeal, the court affirmed the decision. The court held that defendant's arguments based upon false and inaccurate statements were wholly without merit. The court also found that defendant's argument that the sentence of the lower court was altered by the prison authorities and that he was denied the opportunity to prepare a defense because given no preliminary hearing was equally devoid of substance. The court was satisfied that the lower court ably and correctly disposed of every contention advanced by defendant in his petition for writ of habeas corpus.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order.

Goldstein v. School Dist.


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


170 Pa. Super. 451; 87 A.2d 76; 1952 Pa. Super. LEXIS 298


November 20, 1951, Argued

PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals, Nos. 153 and 154, April T., 1951, from orders of Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Jan. T., 1951, Nos. 1520 and 1521, in cases of H. W. Goldstein, trading as Anchor Distributing Company v. School District of Pittsburgh and James P. Kirk, Treasurer, and Same v. City of Pittsburgh and James P. Kirk, Treasurer.

Appeals by taxpayer from deficiency mercantile tax assessments.

Defendants' preliminary objections in the nature of demurrers sustained and orders entered dismissing appeals. Taxpayer appealed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer challenged the decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which dismissed the taxpayer's appeal from deficiency mercantile tax assessments including interest and penalties made against him by appellee school district.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer was formerly the majority shareholder of a company. The company dissolved and the taxpayer carried on the business of the company by himself. As a wholesale dealer in electrical appliances, the taxpayer was subjected to provisions of the Mercantile Tax Resolution adopted and the Regulations promulgated by the School District pursuant to the Act of June 20, 1947, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 24, § 582.1 et seq. and Pittsburgh, Pa., Ordinance 488 enacted under the Act of June 25, 1947, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 53, § 2015.1 et seq. Both pieces of legislation imposed a tax on annual gross business transacted. As an individual, the taxpayer failed to file a new mercantile tax return. The taxpayer argued that he should not have been required to pay interest on the deficiency. The court disagreed. The tax was due the date the return was filed and interest properly began to run as of that date. The court found that the fact that the taxpayer had acted in good faith was irrelevant to the question of whether interest should have been imposed against him. The court further concluded that the mercantile taxes self-assessing nature still amounted to a valid tax.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment.

 

692 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Loushay Appeal


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


169 Pa. Super. 543; 83 A.2d 408; 1951 Pa. Super. LEXIS 458


March 30, 1951, Argued  
September 26, 1951 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals, Nos. 85, 86, and 87, Oct. T., 1951, from judgment of Court of Common Pleas of Bradford County, Sept. T., 1950, Nos. 110 and 189, in re Appeals of J. Lloyd Loushay, John E. Snedeker and R. G. C. Jones from the Auditors' Report of Bradford County.

Appeal by county commissioners from surcharge by county auditors. Before Rosenfield, P.J.

Order entered directing entry of judgment of surcharge against county commissioners jointly and severally. Commissioners appealed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county commissioners challenged the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Bradford County (Pennsylvania), which entered judgment on a surcharge by the county auditors. The surcharge covered payments made to themselves as officers of the county institution district pursuant to Act of May 14, 1949, P.L. 1359.


OVERVIEW: Acting under the amendatory Act of May 14, 1949, P.L. 1359, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 62, § 2253, providing for an additional allowance for expenses incurred in connection with the institution district, the county commissioner paid themselves the sum of $ 500 each, a total of $ 1,500, for the last six months of 1949. Upon the audit of the commissioners' accounts for 1949, the county auditors surcharged them the sum of $ 1,500. In so doing the auditors found that the payment of this money was without authority of any valid law. The court held that a lump sum allowance, or appropriation in gross, for expenses was not per se illegal or contrary to the constitutional prohibition against an increase in salary or emoluments. However, such allowance had to be reasonable to warrant the conclusion that it covered a certified statement of expenses. The greatest expense incurred by the commissioners in connection with the district for any previous year was $ 104.89. A total allowance of $ 3,000 per year was wholly disproportionate and without any reasonable relationship to actual expenses incurred. There was no approach that would permit any other conclusion than that the Act was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

 

693 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Donahue v. Knaus


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


169 Pa. Super. 372; 82 A.2d 532; 1951 Pa. Super. LEXIS 407


April 13, 1951, Submitted  
July 19, 1951 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal, No. 17, April T., 1951, from judgment of Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, July T., 1949, No. 1782, in case of William B. Donahue, Trading as Donahue Furnace Company v. William A. Knaus, Admr., Estate of Anton Knaus, Deceased.

Assumpsit.

Defendant's preliminary objections sustained and judgment entered for defendant, before Marshall, Kennedy and Weiss, JJ., opinion by Marshall, J. Plaintiff appealed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff furnace company contracted with a property owner. The owner died and his estate administrator was defendant. The Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania) sustained the administrator's preliminary objections that more than a year had elapsed between the death of the decedent and the beginning of the action against the administrator and entered judgment for him. The company appealed.


OVERVIEW: The company was to install furnaces in the owner's houses. Prior to the installation the owner was killed. The estate administrator requested installation at a later date. The administrator asked for additional cold air returns not called for in the contract. Upon refusal by the administrator to pay the balance due on the contract or for the extra work, the company sued. The issue was one of first impression whether § 35(b) of the Fiduciaries Act of June 7, 1917, P. L. 447, as amended, extended or restricted the statute of limitations. An amendment prohibited bringing in new parties after the running of the statute of limitations. The court found that no proceedings were taken against the administrator until after the expiration of the year provided for by the statute and not until after the two years provided generally. The court concluded that the company was not barred by any statute of limitations. A person's death did not stop the running of the statute of limitations applicable to any claim against him, but a claim which otherwise was barred within one year after the death of the decedent was not barred until the expiration of one year after his death.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment and remitted the record with direction to the trial court to require the administrator to answer on the merits.

Associates Discount Corp. v. Kelly


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


169 Pa. Super. 74; 82 A.2d 689; 1951 Pa. Super. LEXIS 443


April 10, 1951, Argued  
July 19, 1951 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal, No. 149, Apr. T., 1950, from order of Court of Common Pleas of Beaver County, Sept. T., 1949, No. 271, in case of Associates Discount Corporation v. Herbert Kelly.

Proceeding upon petition of defendant and rule to show cause why judgment entered by confession under bailment lease should not be opened and defendant allowed to plead.

Order entered discharging rule, opinion by McCreary, P. J. Defendant appealed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant lessee challenged an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Beaver County (Pennsylvania), which denied his petition to open a judgment by confession entered in favor of appellee, assignee of lessor, pursuant to a bailment-lease involving a commercial vehicle.


OVERVIEW: A dispute arose between the lessee and the assignee regarding a bailment-lease of a commercial vehicle. The lessee was apparently delinquent in making installment payments. As a result, the assignee sought judgment by confession, as provided for in the lease. Judgment was entered against the lessee, who petitioned to open the same. The lower court denied the petition. On appeal, the court affirmed the lower court's judgment, concluding that the assignee's affidavit of value as to the vehicle was binding only as to the replevin action. The averment was merely an admission for purposes of other actions. Regardless of the affidavit, however, the evidence supported the determination as to the value of the vehicle. Furthermore, the judgment by confession did not violate the Motor Vehicle Sales Finance Act of 1947, Pamphlet Laws 1110, § 27. That Act, as amended, did not apply to the transaction at issue based on the sale price of the vehicle. Thus, it was irrelevant whether the judgment conformed to the Act's requirements. Finally, it was unnecessary to reach the question of whether amendment's to the Act constituted impairments of the obligations of a contract.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed.

Commonwealth ex rel. Carmelo v. Burke

Ann. tit. 61, § 305, and dismissing relator's petition for a writ of habeas corpus. Relator was convicted of attempted robbery and was sentenced to a term of not less than two years nor more than seven years in the state penitentiary. Relator contended that his maximum sentence expired on March 29, 1950, seven years after its effective date. The Pennsylvania Board of Parole, however, calculated the expiration date of his sentence to be May 5, 1951. The court found that when relator was granted a parole, he was simply released from prison on specified conditions; his release was at all times revocable for breach of its conditions with the effect which the Act prescribed for his misconduct. The court found nothing constitutionally objectionable in § 10 of the Act of June 19, 1911, P. L. 1055, as amended, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 61, § 305. The Act of August 6, 1941, P. L. 861, § 17, as amended, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 61, § 331.17, required no comment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order.

 

696 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth ex rel. Brough v. Burke


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


168 Pa. Super. 119; 78 A.2d 25; 1951 Pa. Super. LEXIS 307


September 27, 1950, Argued  
January 12, 1951 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Original jurisdiction. No. 70 Miscellaneous Docket No. 6, petition for writ of habeas corpus, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania ex rel. Russell E. Brough v. C. J. Burke, Warden, Eastern State Penitentiary.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner prisoner presented a petition for a writ of habeas corpus, seeking to be credited on his sentence for the time he was parole.


OVERVIEW: The prisoner contended that he should have been allowed credit on his sentence for the time he was parole. The court determined under § 10 of the Act of June 11, 1911, P.L. 1055, as amended, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 61, § 305, credit for time on parole had to be denied to a parole violator. The court explained that under § 4 of the Act of 1893, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 61, § 523, such credit could have been denied in the discretion of the parole authorities. The court noted that whether the action of the Board of Parole was mandatory or discretionary, the term of the sentence was not extended. The court concluded that notwithstanding the phraseology of § 4, no time was added to the duration of the prisoner's maximum sentence in denying him credit for the time he was on parole and in requiring him to serve the maximum sentence without credit for the period or periods he was on parole. The court determined that a reformatory prisoner who had violated his parole could have been denied credit, in the discretion of the parole authorities, for the time spent on parole in the computation of the duration of his maximum sentence.


OUTCOME: The court discharged the rule to show cause why the writ should not have issued. The court refused the writ of habeas corpus.

Appeal of Clarendon v. F. W. Home Ass'n


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


167 Pa. Super. 44; 75 A.2d 171; 1950 Pa. Super. LEXIS 514


March 20, 1950, Argued  
July 20, 1950 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal, No. 78, April T., 1950, from order of Court of Quarter Sessions of Warren County, Nov. T., 1949, No. 35, in re Appeal of Clarendon V. F. W. Home Association from Refusal of Pennsylvania Liquor Control Board to grant club liquor license.

Proceeding upon motion of Pennsylvania Liquor Control Board and rule to show cause why order entered authorizing a club liquor license to issue to applicant should not be reversed.

Order entered rescinding previous order and affirming decision of Pennsylvania Liquor Control Board refusing application, opinion by Wade, P.J. Applicant appealed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant veterans' club sought review of an order of the Court of Quarter Sessions of Warren County (Pennsylvania), which rescinded a previous order that it had entered, granting the veterans' club a club liquor license, and which affirmed a decision of appellee Pennsylvania Liquor Control Board that refused the club's application.


OVERVIEW: The veterans' club sought a club liquor license from the Board. The Board denied the club license, but the court of quarter sessions granted the veterans' club a liquor license. The Board failed to appeal and refused to issue the license. The following court term the Board sought a rule to show cause why the order should not be reversed, and the court of quarter sessions reversed its earlier decision. On review, the court reversed and reinstated the original decision of the court of quarter sessions. The court noted that after the expiration of the annual term, the court of quarter sessions was without power to reconsider, alter, amend, or to do any act in derogation of its order, adjudication or sentence entered during that term. The rule applied particularly where the time for appeal also had expired. The rule applied even where the purpose of the reconsideration, after the expiration of the term, was to correct an order which was erroneous in law. A litigant could not, by a petition to reconsider an appealable order from which no appeal had been taken within the period allowed by law, reopen the order and thereby nullify the limitation for appeal fixed by statute.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the court of quarter sessions and reinstated that court's original order.

Commonwealth v. Flickinger


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


165 Pa. Super. 95; 67 A.2d 779; 1949 Pa. Super. LEXIS 454


March 21, 1949, Argued  
July 15, 1949 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal, No. 73, Oct. T., 1949, from judgment of Court of Quarter Sessions of Lehigh County, June Sessions, 1948, No. 40, in case of Commonwealth v. Alfred D. Flickinger.

Appeal from conviction by alderman upon charges of possession of untaxed cigarettes in violation of Act of June 21, 1947, P. L. 911. Before Diefenderfer, J., without a jury.

Verdict of guilty and judgment of sentence entered thereon. Defendant appealed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from an order of the Court of Quarter Sessions of Lehigh County (Pennsylvania), which affirmed his conviction by an alderman upon charges of possession of untaxed cigarettes in violation of Act of June 21, 1947, P. L. 911, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 72, § 3167.1. The lower court dismissed his motion for arrest of judgment. Defendant contended the Act was unconstitutional because it unlawfully interfered with interstate commerce.


OVERVIEW: Defendant purchased over 300 cartons of cigarettes in another state. The cigarettes, without tax stamps, were found in the trunk of his car. The trial court found that defendant violated § 3167.1 because he was not a duly licensed dealer and he intended to sell the cigarettes within the state. On appeal, the court held that § 3167.1 was a valid exercise of the State's police powers; it neither created an economic barrier to commerce between the states nor otherwise discriminated against such commerce. A state could enact reasonable laws, under its police powers, relative to interstate commerce, as long as the laws were local in character and only incidentally affected interstate commerce. The court refused to consider defendant's hypothetical examples of how § 3167.1 might burden interstate commerce. According to Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 46, § 552(3), it was presumed that the legislature did not intend to violate the state or federal constitutions. Any doubt regarding a statute's constitutionality had to be resolved in favor of the statute. Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 72, § 3167.1 did not deny defendant equal protection of law or deprive him of property without due process of law.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction and ordered him to appear in the court below to be committed until he had complied with the sentence or any part of it that had not been performed at the time the appeal was made a supersedeas.

Howarth v. Gilman


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


164 Pa. Super. 454; 65 A.2d 691; 1949 Pa. Super. LEXIS 374


October 5, 1948, Argued  
April 20, 1949 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal, No. 117, Oct. T., 1948, from judgment of Municipal Court, Philadelphia Co., Dec. T., 1946, No. 165, in case of John A. Howarth et ux., trading as Howarth & Co., v. George H. Gilman et al., individually and trading as Gilman Mfg. Co.

Assumpsit. Before Bonnelly, J.

Verdict for plaintiff and judgment entered thereon. Defendants appealed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant owners challenged the judgment of the Municipal Court of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which found in favor of appellee engineers in their action to recover for services rendered in preparing plans and drawings for a clothes hamper and for engineering advice concerning the manufacture and production of wardrobe cabinets.


OVERVIEW: The owners argued that the contracts were illegal because the engineers were engaged in the practice of the profession of engineering without a license. They argued that this was contrary to the Act of May 23, 1945, P L. 913, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 63, § 148 et seq. The pivotal issue was whether the engineers were engaged in the practice of engineering within the meaning of the Act. The engineers admitted that they did not register and obtain a license but argued that the Act was not applicable to them. The court disagreed and reversed the judgment. The court found that a mere cursory examination of the multitude of highly technical and intricate exhibits and drawings prepared by the engineers revealed that engineering knowledge was required and employed. The court held that the acts done and the services performed for the owners were within the scope of the legislative enactment and that the Act of 1945 was a valid and reasonable exercise of the police power. Thus, the engineers were precluded from recovery.


OUTCOME: The judgment in favor of the engineers was reversed and entered for the owners.

 

701 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Klick


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


164 Pa. Super. 449; 65 A.2d 440; 1949 Pa. Super. LEXIS 369


March 14, 1949, Argued  
April 13, 1949 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal, No. 12, March T., 1949, from judgment of Quarter Sessions, Perry Co., Nov. Sessions, 1948, No. 9, in case of Commonwealth v. Luther F. Klick.

Information charging defendant with violation of Vehicle Code of 1929. Before Rice, P. J.

Defendant's demurrer to the evidence overruled and judgment of sentence entered. Defendant appealed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the decision of the Court of Quarter Sessions, Perry County (Pennsylvania), which found him guilty of operating a motor vehicle in violation of § 1002(a), Art. X, of The Vehicle Code of May 1, 1929, P. L. 905 (Pennsylvania), as amended, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 75, § 501.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was found guilty of speeding. On review, the court affirmed. Official signs as to permissible maximum speed could not relieve one from a violation of § 1002(a), Art. X, of The Vehicle Code of May 1, 1929, P. L. 905 (Pennsylvania), as amended, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 75, § 501, merely by driving within the speed limit prescribed. The fact that the information charged defendant with operation of his automobile in a residence district at a speed in excess of the maximum indicated by official signs did not take the prosecution out of § 1002(a). The testimony of a peace officer that he timed defendant's speed using a motor vehicle for the purpose, equipped with a speedometer which had been tested for accuracy within 30 days, was competent and sufficient proof of the speed at which defendant was driving. That method of determining speed was not restricted to violations on the open highway or outside of a business or a residence district. The ordinary person could understand what standard of conduct was imposed, as well as what he may do and what was forbidden under § 1002(a).


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

Hartman v. Columbia Malleable Castings Corp.


Superior Court of Pennsylvania

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff employee sought review of a decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Lancaster County (Pennsylvania), which directed a verdict in favor of defendant employer in the employee's action of assumpsit brought to recover money that was withheld by the employer in accordance with the provisions of § 20 of the Pa. Local Tax Collection Law of May 25, 1945, P. L. 1050.


OVERVIEW: The money that was withheld by the employer represented a tax that was levied by a school district. The tax was levied against both the employee and his wife. After the trial court directed a verdict in favor of the employer, the employee appealed. The court held that the employee was not denied his constitutional rights because of an absence of notice and hearing. The method of collection involved was valid and met all the requirement of due process. The employee contended that he was not liable for the taxes as levied against his wife. The court determined that it could fairly be assumed that the legislative intent was that the employee was responsible for the taxes of his wife because he had a legal duty to provide for the wife's support and maintenance.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment.

Wilkes-Barre v. Pennsylvania Public Utility Com.


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


164 Pa. Super. 210; 63 A.2d 452; 1949 Pa. Super. LEXIS 315


October 8, 1948, Argued  
January 14, 1949 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals, Nos. 127 and 128, Oct. T., 1947, from order of Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission, dated February 17, 1947, Complaint Docket No. 13592, in case of City of Wilkes-Barre et al. v. Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission.

Proceedings before Public Utility Commission as to condition of a bridge in Wilkes-Barre.

Order entered closing bridge and directing certain repairs and allocating costs thereof among parties concerned. City and county, respectively, appealed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, city, county and railroads, sought review of an order of the Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission, which closed a bridge and directed its repair, allocating costs among appellants. Appellants argued that they were not responsible for the costs of repair, which were to be borne by the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, under The Act of May 23, 1945, 36 P.S. § 105 (1945), amending the Act of June 22, 1931, 1931 Pa. Laws 720, § 4.


OVERVIEW: The Commission ordered the Department of Highways to reconstruct a bridge over a railroad crossing in Wilkes-Barre, and allocated the costs to appellants. Appellants objected to the allocation of costs, asserting that the Commonwealth was bound by the Act of May 23, 1945 to pay all such costs for bridges, and sought review in the court. The court affirmed the order of the Commission, holding that the Act had not repealed the Public Utility Law, which permitted the Commission to regulate construction of utility crossings. Specifically, the court held that there was no such repeal because there was no express amendment of the Public Utility Law contained in the Act, which amendment would in any event be unconstitutional. The court further held that the Act amended the law regarding improvements of only those bridges on state highways, which were not under the purview of the Commission. Therefore, the court held that because the bridge in question was a public utility crossing, the Commission had the authority to order the repairs and allocate costs.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the Commission's order to reconstruct a bridge over a railroad crossing and its allocation of costs to appellants.

Phila. v. Watt


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


162 Pa. Super. 433; 57 A.2d 591; 1948 Pa. Super. LEXIS 469


October 6, 1947, Argued  
March 16, 1948 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal, No. 153, Oct. T., 1947, from judgment of C. P. No. 7, Phila. Co., June T., 1946, No. 4364, in case of Philadelphia, to use, State Paving and Construction Co. v. Alexander H. Watt et ux.

Scire facias sur municipal claim proceeding. Before Kun, J., without a jury.

Adjudication filed finding for plaintiff; exceptions to adjudication dismissed and judgment entered for plaintiff. Defendants appealed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant property owners appealed a judgment from the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which was rendered for plaintiff, the City of Philadelphia, Pennsylvania, and use-plaintiff contractor in a scire facias sur municipal claim proceeding to recover the costs of paving a private driveway that abutted the owners' property, pursuant to the Act of May 17, 1939, P. L. 151, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 53, § 3681 et seq.


OVERVIEW: A strip of the owners' land was part of a private driveway that connected two public streets. Pursuant to the Act, the City's board of health notified the owners of a resolution in which the driveway was declared a nuisance because of improper grading and defective paving. The owners, and other adjoining landowners, were directed to pave the driveway with concrete within 10 days. When neither the owners nor other landowners responded, the City hired the contractor to repave the driveway. The owners were assessed their proportionate cost of the improvement, and a municipal claim was filed against their land for the assessment. The contractor recovered a judgment in a scire facias proceeding. On appeal, the court rejected the owners' claim that the Act unconstitutionally violated their due process rights. The nature and quality of the board's authority to abate a nuisance was unquestionable, the nuisance was established, and the board could not be charged with an abuse of discretion in so declaring. The owners were given an opportunity to be heard, and in the absence of proof to the contrary, the court assumed that paving with concrete was reasonably necessary to abate the nuisance.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment.

 

705 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Boyertown Burial Casket Co. v. Unemployment Compensation Board of Review


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


162 Pa. Super. 98; 56 A.2d 390; 1948 Pa. Super. LEXIS 445; 21 L.R.R.M. 2403


September 30, 1947, Argued  
January 8, 1948 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal, No. 119, Oct. T., 1947, by employer, Boyertown Burial Casket Company, from Decision (No. B-44-98-B-330) of Unemployment Compensation Board of Review, dated Feb. 10, 1947, affirming decision of Referee, affirming decision of Unemployment Compensation Bureau allowing compensation to claimant, Russell R. Albright. Decision affirmed; reargument refused January 27, 1948.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employer sought review of the decision of the Unemployment Compensation Board of Review (Pennsylvania), which affirmed the referee's decision of the Unemployment Compensation Bureau allowing compensation to claimant employee. The employer contended that the employee should not have been granted unemployment compensation for a voluntary suspension of work during an industrial dispute.


OVERVIEW: The employee and his associates went out on strike after negotiations with the employer failed. They were awarded unemployment compensation after the waiting period under Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 43, § 802, which provided compensation for unemployment due to a voluntary suspension of work resulting from an industrial dispute. The court found that the wisdom of the statute was not for the judicial branch unless prohibited by the constitution. The court determined that the statute was constitutional, notwithstanding the employer's attacks based on the increase of taxes or contributions under the "experience rating" provisions of Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 43, § 781. The court rejected the employer's contention that benefits should have been denied on the theory that the settlement of the labor dispute immediately upon the expiration of benefits showed a lack of good faith because nothing in the act deprived the employee of compensation for such reason. The court concluded that the amendment of the act to provide a reward or adjustment of the employer's contribution under an "experience rating" formula did not render the policy of paying compensation in industrial dispute cases unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the board's decision.

 

706 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Vanport v. Brobeck


No. 702 C.D. 1975


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


22 Pa. Commw. 523; 349 A.2d 523; 1975 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1364


October 31, 1975, Argued  
December 23, 1975, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Beaver County in case of Township of Vanport v. Harold W. Brobeck, No. 1492 of 1974.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant offender challenged the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Beaver County (Pennsylvania), which found appellant guilty of violating § 814 of the Zoning Ordinance of appellee Township of Vanport and imposed a fine.


OVERVIEW: Appellant offender was found guilty of violating § 814 of the Zoning Ordinance of appellee Township of Vanport, which prohibited the use of lots or premises for junk storage or garbage dumps. Appellant challenged the trial court's judgment on several grounds and the court affirmed. In response to appellant's claim that § 814, a penal regulation, should be strictly construed, the court found that the trial court properly construed the ordinance and placed the appropriate burden of proof upon appellee. Regarding appellant's claim that § 814 unconstitutionally delegated General Assembly powers and did not require that a nuisance be found to exist, the court ruled that state police power authorized the government to regulate land use. The court further found that the Pennsylvania Municipalities Planning Code, 53 P.S. § 10603 (1968), permitted local legislative bodies to regulate public health, safety, and welfare through the use of zoning ordinances. Finally, the trial court's fine imposed on de novo review that was greater than the district court's fine was not an abuse of discretion, and thus, there was no error.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which found appellant offender guilty of violating appellee Township of Vanport's ordinance and imposed a fine. The trial court properly construed the ordinance; the ordinance was a valid exercise of police power to regulate public health, safety, and welfare; and the trial court did not abuse its discretion in increasing the amount of appellant's fine.

 

707 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth Ins. Dep't v. Straff


No. 475 C.D. 1975


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


22 Pa. Commw. 419; 349 A.2d 525; 1975 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1348


December 4, 1975, Argued  
December 22, 1975, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Reargument Filed and Denied.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania Insurance Department in case of In the Matter of: Paul Straff, Public Adjuster, No. P74-8-3.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant public adjuster licensee filed a motion of appeal from the order and adjudication of the Department of Insurance (Pennsylvania), that revoked his adjuster's license, on the grounds of insufficient evidence and the unconstitutionality of the act in question.


OVERVIEW: Appellant's public adjusters license was revoked by appellee Insurance Department. On appeal from that administrative proceeding, appellant argued that the record did not support findings that appellant was untrustworthy; that it was error for the Commissioner to not state his reason for invoking revocation; and that the act in question was unconstitutionally vague. The court affirmed the judgment holding the record did reveal that evidence did support the conclusion of the Commissioner. It also held that Real Estate Brokers' License Act, Act of May 1, 1929, Pub. L. 1216 § 10, as amended, 63 Pa. Stat. § 440, provided that the Commissioner shall revoke the license unless, in his discretion, he decided to invoke a fine. Because the Commissioner did not invoke a fine, the Commissioner was not required to give his reasoning. Finally, it held that appellant's vagueness argument was without merit.


OUTCOME: Order of appellee Insurance Department that revoked appellant's public adjusters license was affirmed where the findings were supported by the evidence, and the Commissioner was not required to indicate his reasoning in a case where he did not invoke a fine in sanctioning, and because appellant's vagueness argument was without merit.

 

708 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth, Dep't of Education v. First School


No. 1562 C.D. 1974


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


22 Pa. Commw. 334; 348 A.2d 458; 1975 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1330


June 5, 1975, Argued  
December 12, 1975, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargued September 10, 1975.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Order of the Secretary of Education in case of In Re: Application of The First School under Act 109 of 1968, dated November 15, 1974.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant school sought review of an order by the Pennsylvania secretary of education, who refused funding requested by appellant pursuant to 24 P.S. § 5601 et seq. (1968) for reimbursement for expenditures for teachers' salaries, text books, and other instructional materials.


OVERVIEW: Appellant school sought review of an order refusing it funding requested pursuant to the Nonpublic Elementary and Secondary Education Act, 24 P.S. § 5601 et seq. (1968) (the Act) for reimbursement for expenditures for teachers' salaries, text books, and other instructional materials. The court reversed the decision and directed appellant's application be processed in accordance with the Act, holding that the Act was not unconstitutional with reference to nonpublic, nonsectarian schools. The court found that the Act was readily capable of being administered along a sectarian-nonsectarian dichotomy because the reimbursements under the Act went directly to the schools rather than to parents, and it was originally administered with this dichotomy in mind. The court held the act favored severability in order to benefit nonsectarian schools regardless of the fact that the Act was found to be unconstitutional with regard to sectarian schools. Finally, the court found that the Act was not repealed by 24 P.S. § 9-972 (1972) or 24 P.S. § 9-973 (1972) because while there was some duplication among the statutes, the legislature did not intend to eliminate the aid provided for in the Act.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded an order refusing appellant school requested funding under a statute providing for reimbursement for instructional materials because the statute was not unconstitutional with reference to nonpublic, nonsectarian schools and the statute was severable with reference to these schools.

Benham v. Board of Supervisors


No. 272 C.D. 1975


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


22 Pa. Commw. 245; 349 A.2d 484; 1975 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1314


October 7, 1975, Argued  
December 8, 1975, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County in case of Grant J. Benham, Paul Darlington and Richard S. Darlington v. Board of Supervisors of Middletown Township and John C. Snyder, et al. and William L. Potter, et al. No. 7562 of 1974.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landowners sought review of a decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County (Pennsylvania), which upheld a decision of appellee township not to adopt a curative amendment proposed by appellants to remedy an alleged unconstitutional prohibition on the use of property within appellee township. Appellants also challenged the decision to permit appellee intervenors to participate in the lower court proceedings.


OVERVIEW: Appellant landowners wanted to build townhouses in appellee township, on land within a single unit residential district. They filed an application with appellee for a curative amendment to the zoning ordinance that challenged the constitutionality of an alleged prohibition against townhouses. The lower court affirmed appellee's the denial. On appeal, the court affirmed because appellants had not proved that the ordinance completely or effectively banned the requested use. The court noted that townhouses were permitted in three zoning areas and under a planned residential development law, and substantial evidence supported the trial court's conclusion that large lot zoning was not prohibitive of townhouse development. The court found that the zoning laws were not designed to selectively exclude newcomers and appellant's complaint that the lower court should have conducted a trial de novo was belied by 53 P.S. § 11010, which provided that appellee's findings of fact could not be disturbed if supported by substantial evidence. The court held there was no confiscatory taking, and intervention by interested parties in the appeal was permitted by 53 P.S. § 11009.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision which affirmed appellee township's denial of a curative amendment to the zoning ordinance, and permitted appellee intervenors to participate in the lower court proceedings, because the intervention was permitted by statute and appellant landowners had not proven that townhouses were completely prohibited or restricted in order to improperly close the community's doors to growth.

United States Tobacco Co. v. Commonwealth


No. 1516 C.D. 1974


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


22 Pa. Commw. 211; 348 A.2d 755; 1975 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1385


September 8, 1975, Argued  
December 5, 1975, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Board of Finance and Revenue in case of Petition of United States Tobacco Company, Docket No. R-774.



DISPOSITION: Judgment entered for Commonwealth and against taxpayer.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant corporation challenged the judgment of the Board of Finance and Revenue (Pennsylvania), which sustained the order of the Settlement Board, which assessed state corporate income tax against appellant. Appellant argued that assessment of tax on interstate sales violated 15 U.S.C.S. § 381 et seq., and the U.S. Constitution, and that the add back of corporate income tax after the apportionment of Pennsylvania activity was invalid.


OVERVIEW: The Settlement Board assessed income taxes against appellant corporation. Appellant challenged the assessment at the Board of Finance and Revenue (board), which affirmed. Appellant challenged the board's judgment, and argued that the assessment of taxes on its interstate sales violated 15 U.S.C.S. § 381 et seq., and the U.S. Constitution, and that the add back of corporate income tax after the apportionment of income related to sales within the state was invalid. The court affirmed. The court held that evidence that appellant's representatives made calls on business within the state to promote appellant's products was sufficient to show that appellant was doing business within the state and to subject it to taxation. The court held that the add back of income tax after apportionment was valid and in accordance with 72 P.S. §§ 7401(3)(1)-(2).


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the tax assessment against appellant corporation. The court held that appellant was subject to taxes because the presence of its representatives in the state provided a sufficient nexus to the state. The court held that the add back of income taxes to the amount of income apportioned to sales within the state was proper.

Faust v. Police Civil Service Com.


No. 424 C.D. 1975


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


22 Pa. Commw. 123; 347 A.2d 765; 1975 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1294


September 11, 1975, Argued  
November 26, 1975, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Centre County in case of Lee W. Faust v. Police Civil Service Commission of Borough of State College, dated September 27, 1974.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant police officer challenged the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Centre County (Pennsylvania), which sustained appellee police commission's order dismissing appellant for immorality and conduct unbecoming an officer in appellant's action challenging his dismissal. Appellant argued that there was insufficient evidence of conduct unbecoming an officer and that the charges filed against him were unconstitutional for vagueness.


OVERVIEW: Appellant police officer, a married man, had sexual relations with a married woman in a motel room and at a public park. He was advised by a superior and agreed to cease relations with the woman. The relations resumed days later, and appellant was suspended and served with a statement of charges deemed as immorality and conduct unbecoming an officer. Appellee police commission issued an order dismissing appellant. The trial court upheld the order. On appeal, the court affirmed the dismissal and held that in applying the community standard, appellant's adultery, committed while not in uniform and off duty in a public motel and a public park, was immorality and conduct unbecoming an officer under 53 P.S. § 46190(4). Police officers were held to a higher standard of conduct, and the state had a compelling interest in maintaining a high degree of public respect. Adultery by appellant, whether in private or in public, affected the morale of the department and reduced appellant's effectiveness in the community. Further, the charges did not violate the vagueness doctrine because appellant was advised and agreed to cease the adulterous conduct days prior to the suspension.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order, which upheld appellee police commission's order dismissing appellant police officer because his adulterous conduct committed off duty in a public motel and at a public park constituted immorality and conduct unbecoming an officer. Further, the supervisor's warning to appellant to cease the adulterous conduct days prior to the suspension was adequate notice of the charges and satisfied the vagueness doctrine.

 

715 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Griffie


No. 1439 C.D. 1974


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


21 Pa. Commw. 403; 346 A.2d 838; 1975 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1214


June 6, 1975, Argued  
October 23, 1975, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Cumberland County in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Ray Earl Griffie, No. 90 May Term, 1974.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant operator challenged an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Cumberland County (Pennsylvania), which affirmed the suspension of appellant's driving privileges by appellee state. Appellant contended that his arrest was unlawful because the arresting officer, a policeman for the Borough of Carlisle, lacked authority to cross the borough line into North Middleton Township to make an arrest in pursuit of a misdemeanant.


OVERVIEW: Appellant operator was arrested for driving while intoxicated. When he refused to submit to a breath test, his driving privileges were suspended under 75 P.S. § 624.1(a). He unsuccessfully appealed, arguing that the arresting officer was a borough policeman and lacked statutory authority under 19 P.S. § 11 (amended 1973) to arrest him outside the borough's boundary. On appeal, appellant argued that § 624.1(a) was unconstitutional in not requiring a lawful arrest in order to request a driver to submit to a breath test. The court disagreed, noting that appellant's arrest was unlawful only due to a statutory limitation on the officer's jurisdiction, which did not apply to license suspension proceedings as a matter of state law. The officer observed appellant driving erratically on a borough roadway before stopping him a few blocks away outside borough limits. Appellant's breath smelled of alcohol, and he admitted having had a few drinks. The court affirmed the lower court's finding that the officer had reasonable grounds to believe appellant was driving while intoxicated, and held that the reviewing court did not err in considering the officer's observations after stopping appellant.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellee state's suspension of appellant operator's driver's license, holding that the lawfulness of neither the borough officer's stop nor his arrest of appellant was significant in this case, because the purpose was to request appellant, who was suspected of driving under the influence of intoxicating liquor, to submit to a breathalyzer test.

OVERVIEW: The Public Utility Commission issued a ruling that would have allowed appellee electric company to bill appellant trustees of a bankrupt company's property on a biweekly rather than monthly basis, and appellants sought review.
