Mobil Oil Co. v. Westtown


No. 1134 C.D. 1974


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


21 Pa. Commw. 295; 345 A.2d 313; 1975 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1189


April 2, 1975, Argued  
October 2, 1975, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Reargument Filed and Denied.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Chester County in case of In Re: Appeal of Mobile Oil Company and Hibbard Bartram, No. 83 September Term, 1972.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant township challenged the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Chester County (Pennsylvania) that directed appellant to issue a building permit to appellees, property owner and lessee, for the construction of a gasoline service station.


OVERVIEW: Appellees, property owner and lessee, were denied zoning approval for the construction of a gasoline service station. On appeal, the trial court ordered appellant township to issue appellees a building permit. The court reversed and reinstated the order of the zoning hearing board, holding that the permit was properly denied on the basis of appellees' failure to comply with the 500-foot spacing requirement, found in Westtown Township, Pa., Zoning Ordinance § 301(k). The ordinance prohibited two service stations from being located within 500 feet of one another on the same side of the road. The court observed that there were many locations still available where the restriction would not present a problem. Moreover, the court found that 500 feet was not an unduly restrictive spacing requirement because two of the three existing commercial districts were located along major traffic arteries. Therefore, the court concluded that it was within the proper exercise of appellant's police power to limit access and to attempt to reduce the number of accidents, which might otherwise be caused by cars entering or leaving the roadway.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order that directed appellant township to issue a building permit to appellees, property owner and lessee, for the construction of a gasoline service station. The court reinstated the zoning hearing board's order, insofar as it denied appellees' application for a special exception and building permit on the basis of their failure to comply with the zoning ordinance's 500-foot spacing requirement.

 

718 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Guentter v. Lansdale


No. 1308 C.D. 1974


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


21 Pa. Commw. 287; 345 A.2d 306; 1975 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1188


April 3, 1975, Argued  
October 1, 1975, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County in case of Robert F. Guentter and D. Gehr Guentter, his wife; Aaron Diamond and Ester Diamond, his wife; Clinton Toewe and Carol Toewe, his wife; Joseph R. Caltabiano, et al. and Francis Emert, et al. v. Borough of Lansdale, Montgomery County, Pennsylvania, No. 74-01019.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landowners sought review of the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County (Pennsylvania) that upheld the validity of a zoning ordinance enacted by appellee borough.


OVERVIEW: Appellee borough denied appellant landowners' petition, which requested a change in zoning from residential to professional. On appeal, the court affirmed the lower court's judgment that upheld appellee's decision. Appellants challenged the zoning ordinance based on their assertion that, by refusing to rezone, appellee was engaging in "reverse spot zoning." The court noted that the singling out of one lot or a small area for different treatment from that accorded to similar surrounding land indistinguishable from it in character, for the economic benefit of the owner or to his economic detriment, was invalid "spot" zoning. However, the court found that the land in question was not surrounded by land that was being treated differently. Rather, it was bounded on the one side by a residential district and on the other side by a professional office district. At the zoning hearing there was an honest difference of opinion expressed as to how the land should be zoned. Therefore, the court could not disturb the legislative decision because the validity of the legislation was fairly debatable, and the legislative judgment controlled.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that upheld the validity of a zoning ordinance enacted by appellee borough. The court rejected appellant landowners' assertion that appellee was engaged in "reverse spot zoning" when it refused to rezone the land from residential to a professional district. The court could not disturb the legislative decision where the validity of the legislation was fairly debatable.

 

719 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Staley


No. 767 C.D. 1974


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


21 Pa. Commw. 193; 344 A.2d 748; 1975 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1382


June 5, 1975, Argued  
September 9, 1975, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Board of Finance and Revenue in case of In Re: Appeal of Fred W. Staley and Barbara K. Staley, No. RPIT-96.



DISPOSITION: Appeal dismissed. Judgment entered accordingly.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayers sought review of the decision of the Board of Finance and Revenue (Pennsylvania), which opted not to review the order of the Department of Revenue (Pennsylvania), which refused to allow appellants a reduction in gross income for unreimbursed business expenses.


OVERVIEW: Appellant taxpayers filed a joint return claiming a reduction in gross income for unreimbursed business expenses. The Commonwealth's Department of Revenue refused to allow the deduction and issued an assessment for taxes owed. The Commonwealth's Board of Finance and Revenue refused appellants' petition for review of the department's decision. The court dismissed the appeal, and held that appellants could not deduct unreimbursed business expenses from taxable income, because 72 P. S. § 7301(d) was clear on its face and could not be construed to allow such a deduction. The court found that § 7301's provision for excluding payments to reimburse actual business expenses could not be construed to also allow deduction of unreimbursed expense from taxable income. The court held that the refusal to allow the deduction was not unconstitutional under the uniformity clause of the Pa. Const., art. VIII, § 1, because the classification of taxpayers subject to the tax was reasonable and the tax itself was applied equally within the class.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the appeal of appellant taxpayers and held that a statute that allowed income exclusions for payment of reimbursed business expenses was clear on its face and could not be construed to also allow a deduction from taxable income for unreimbursed business expenses.

 

720 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Triumph Hosiery Mills, Inc. v. Commonwealth


No. 1609 C.D. 1973


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


21 Pa. Commw. 186; 343 A.2d 710; 1975 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1381


June 5, 1975, Argued  
September 5, 1975, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Board of Finance and Revenue in case of Appeal of Triumph Hosiery Mills, Inc., No. R-52.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment entered accordingly.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, a New York corporation, sought review of the order the Board of Finance and Revenue (Pennsylvania), which affirmed the determination of the Department of Revenue and settled the tax due from appellant under Article IV of the Tax Reform Code of 1971, 72 P.S. § 7401 et seq., at a higher amount.


OVERVIEW: After appellant, a New York corporation, filed its Pennsylvania corporate net income tax return, the Department of Revenue disagreed with the method employed by appellant in the computation of its tax liability, and settled the tax due to a higher amount. The Board of Finance and Revenue affirmed the determination. On appeal, the court affirmed the order below. The court adopted the commonwealth's interpretation of §§ 401(3)(1), 401(3)(2), of Article IV of the Tax Reform Code of 1971, 72 P.S. § 7401 et seq. The court held that when computing its tax base for purposes of the Pennsylvania corporate net income tax, a corporation whose entire business was not transacted in Pennsylvania may apportion its taxable income except insofar as said taxable income represented the "add-back" of that corporation's federal tax deduction for the Pennsylvania corporate net income tax. The failure of the Pennsylvania corporate net income tax to provide for the apportionment of the federal deduction for said tax was not unconstitutional and, in fact, guaranteed equality of treatment to both local and multistate corporations subject to the tax.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the Board of Finance and Revenue settling the tax due from appellant, a New York corporation, under the Tax Reform Code at a higher amount because the "add-back" of the federal deduction for the Pennsylvania corporate net income tax could not be apportioned in computing appellant's Pennsylvania taxable income and such proscription was not unconstitutional.

 

721 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Pa. Parent Assistance Auth. v. Sloan


No. 983 C.D. 1975


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


21 Pa. Commw. 154; 343 A.2d 706; 1975 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1165


August 5, 1975, Argued  
September 4, 1975, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Original jurisdiction in case of Pennsylvania Parent Assistance Authority v. Grace Sloan, Treasurer of the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania.



DISPOSITION: Motion denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff Pennsylvania Parent Assistance Authority sought preliminary and permanent injunctions against defendant Treasurer of the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania to prevent defendant from transferring money from the Parent Reimbursement Fund as mandated by Pa. Act No. 18 and to direct defendant to honor any vouchers submitted by plaintiff for its administrative purposes.


OVERVIEW: Pennsylvania's Parent Reimbursement Act for Non-Public Education, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 24, § 5701, was found unconstitutional by the United States Supreme Court. As a result, plaintiff Pennsylvania Parent Assistance Authority was unable to reimburse parents who sent their children to non-public schools. The governor of Pennsylvania approved Act No. 18, which placed monies that that were in the Parent Reimbursement Fund into the commonwealth's general fund. Plaintiff was the fiduciary of the Parent Reimbursement Fund. Plaintiff sought to enjoin defendant Treasurer of the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania from transferring the monies in the Fund and to have defendant pay plaintiff's administrative vouchers. The court denied plaintiff's motion. The court found that ongoing federal litigation of matters involving the Parent Reimbursement Act did not justify a clear conclusion that any parents would have been entitled to reimbursements. Dissipation of the Fund did not mean parents would not get reimbursements. Plaintiff offered no evidence as to administrative vouchers.


OUTCOME: The court denied plaintiff Pennsylvania Parent Assistance Authority's motion for preliminary and permanent injunctions. The court found that it was unlikely that parents of non-public school children would be entitled to the relief sought, and plaintiff made no showing of administrative expenses.

Commonwealth, Public Utility Com. v. Pennsylvania Radio Tel. Corp.

Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


20 Pa. Commw. 591; 342 A.2d 489; 1975 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1133

August 1, 1975, Decided

DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant corporation sought review of the order of appellee Public Utility Commission (Pennsylvania), which granted a certificate of public convenience to an applicant to provide mobile common carrier service to the public.

The court held that the applicant did not satisfy its burden of establishing all three prerequisites for issuance of a certificate of public convenience.

Raum v. Bd. of Supervisors


Nos. 1322, 1323 and 1386 C.D. 1974


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


20 Pa. Commw. 426; 342 A.2d 450; 1975 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1110


February 3, 1975, Argued  
July 25, 1975, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Reargument Filed and Denied.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Orders of the Court of Common Pleas of Chester County, in cases of Ernest P. Raum, Robert F. Esslinger and Citizens Organized to Reclaim Chesterbrook, an unincorporated association, by Emanuel Lauria and Phyllis Ayoob, Trustees Ad Litem, v. The Board of Supervisors of Tredyffrin Township, Misc. No. 74, 1972; and Appeal of Ernest P. Raum, Robert F. Esslinger, and Citizens Organized to Reclaim Chesterbrook, an unincorporated association, by Emanuel Lauria and Phyllis Ayoob, Trustees Ad Litem (from the decision of the Zoning Hearing Board of Tredyffrin Township, v. Zoning Hearing Board of Tredyffrin Township, No. 19 June Term, 1973.



DISPOSITION: Dismissal of appeal of intervenor affirmed. Order of lower court otherwise reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landowner-developers and intervening protestants, in a consolidated appeal, sought review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Chester County (Pennsylvania), which invalidated an ordinance of appellee town and board, granting appellant landowner-developers preliminary approval of a development and dismissed the appeal of appellant intervening protestors.


OVERVIEW: Appellee town and board passed an ordinance giving appellant landowner-developers preliminary approval for a planned development. Appellant intervening protestors appealed the ordinance. The zoning board upheld the ordinance, and the trial court reversed, invalidated the ordinance, and denied standing to appellant intervening protestors. The court reversed as to the ordinance and affirmed the dismissal of appellant intervening protestors' appeal. The court held that the ordinance was specific and adequately guided the appropriate bodies of appellee town in considering future applications for zoning approvals within the planned development. The court held that the ordinance was valid as conforming with the comprehensive plan, provided sufficient standards to guide the approval of developments thereunder, was authorized under Article VI of the Municipalities Planning Code, 53 P.S. § 10601 et seq., and reflected a reasonable consideration of the environmental impact of the uses permitted thereunder. The court held that appellee intervening protestors did have standing to challenge the ordinance but held that the ordinance was not exclusionary.


OUTCOME: Order invalidating ordinance reversed because the ordinance was specific enough to guide appellee town in considering future zoning approvals, the ordinance conformed with the comprehensive plan, it was authorized under the applicable statute, and considered the environmental impact of the development plan. Order dismissing the appeal of appellee intervening protestors affirmed because the ordinance was not exclusionary.

 

724 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Workmen's Compensation Appeal Board v. Overmyer Mold Co.


No. 1688 C.D. 1974


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


20 Pa. Commw. 456; 342 A.2d 439; 1975 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1112


May 9, 1975, Argued  
July 25, 1975, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the order of the Workmen's Compensation Appeal Board in case of William H. Collier v. Overmyer Mold Company, No. A-68687.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Employer and its insurer challenged a decision from the Workmen's Compensation Appeal Board (Pennsylvania), which affirmed the referee's finding and directed employer to reimburse claimant for medical services furnished by a chiropractor.


OVERVIEW: Claimant employee suffered a compensable injury. Unhappy with the treatment he received from a physician designated by appellant employer, claimant procured treatment from a chiropractor. The court recognized a change in the statute and indicated the new provision made little change in the process. Employer was to furnish medical services. If employer failed to do so, employee could procure other services at employer's expense. Employee could was entitled to reimbursement for self-obtained services only if the medical services provided by employer were inadequate. The court found that treatment by the chiropractor was not authorized by either the designated physician or by the employer. Claimant was required to select a physician from among those designated by employer. Since claimant had actual knowledge of employer's designation at the time he sought self-obtained treatment, he was not entitled to reimbursement and the decision was reversed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision which required employer to reimburse claimant for chiropractic services which claimant obtained because chiropractor was not a physician designated by employer and the treatment had not been authorized by either a designated physician or by employer.

 

725 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth, Dep't of Transp., Bureau of Traffic Safety v. Rodgers


No. 1573 C.D. 1974


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


20 Pa. Commw. 393; 341 A.2d 917; 1975 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 706


June 4, 1975, Argued  
July 22, 1975, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Chester County in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. David V. Rodgers, No. 215 Misc. Term, 1974.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Chester County (Pennsylvania), which suspended his operator's license and vehicle registrations under the financial responsibility laws, 75 P.S. § 1401 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was in a motor vehicle accident in Colorado. A default judgment was entered against him in a Colorado court. Appellant alleged he never received any notice of the action other than from the Secretary of Transportation (Secretary). The Secretary notified appellant that his license and registration would be suspended under the Vehicle Code, 75 P.S. § 1413, for failure to satisfy the judgment. The lower court affirmed the secretary's suspension of appellant's license. Appellant challenged the ruling, contending the suspension based solely on the default judgment, without any evidence of negligence, was a denial of due process and an abuse of discretion. The court affirmed the lower court ruling. The purpose of § 1413 was not to punish negligent drivers, but to protect the public from the financial irresponsibility of those who have had judgments entered against them from motor vehicle accidents. This was a legitimate state interest. Once the claim was reduced to a judgment, there was no requirement for a hearing as to negligence before license revocation. Under the U.S. Constitution, the state accorded full faith and credit to the Colorado judgment.


OUTCOME: The order suspending appellant's operator's license was affirmed because a state statute protected the public from financial irresponsibility from those with judgments against them from motor vehicle accidents, and a Colorado judgment was accorded full faith and credit under the U.S. Constitution.

Brown v. Workmen's Compensation Appeal Board


No. 572 C.D. 1974


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


20 Pa. Commw. 330; 342 A.2d 134; 1975 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 700


June 6, 1975, Argued  
July 17, 1975, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Workmen's Compensation Appeal Board in case of Barbara Brown, Widow of Almer Brown, deceased, v. Connelly Containers, Inc., No. A-64634.



DISPOSITION: Denial order reversed. Case remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant claimant sought review of a judgment of appellee Workmen's Compensation Appeal Board (Pennsylvania), which denied workmen's compensation benefits to appellant's illegitimate child who was born following the death of appellant's boyfriend, an employee of appellee employer.


OVERVIEW: Appellant claimant gave birth to a child three months following the death of her boyfriend, an employee of appellee employer with whom she lived. Respondent Workmen's Compensation Appeal Board affirmed the denial of benefits to the child on the ground that the minor child could neither be deemed to be dependent on the employee nor a member of his household as contemplated by 77 P.S. § 562. The court reversed, holding that illegitimacy per se did not disqualify a child from receiving workmen's compensation benefits and that the term "posthumous," as used in § 562, included posthumous illegitimate children within its meaning inasmuch as the statute suffered from no facial unconstitutionality with respect to illegitimates.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the denial of benefits by respondent Workmen's Compensation Appeal Board to appellant claimant's illegitimate child, holding that posthumous illegitimate children were entitled to workmen's compensation benefits as they were included within the meaning of the statutory term "posthumous."

Warren Sand & Gravel Co. v. Commonwealth, Dep't of Environmental Resources

Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


20 Pa. Commw. 186; 341 A.2d 556; 1975 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1090


March 5, 1975, Argued  
July 9, 1975, Decided

DISPOSITION: Affirmed.

OVERVIEW: Appellant gravel companies, were issued dredging permits with certain conditions attached by the Pennsylvania Department of Environmental Resources (department). On appeal, the Environmental Hearing Board (board) reversed two of the conditions and upheld one condition. Both parties appealed, and the court affirmed, holding that the department had authority to sell the sand and gravel in question pursuant to § 1908-A(3) of the Administrative Code, 71 P.S. § 510-8 (Supp. 1974-1975). The court held that the department had authority to regulate dredging activities pursuant to § 1908-A(1)(d), and § 2 of the Water Obstructions Act, 32 P.S. § 682, and that the permits, including the approved terms and conditions, were a valid exercise of that authority. The court held that the board properly reviewed the exercise of authority by the department and properly considered all of the factors involved, including both environmental harm and economic impact. The court held that all of the findings of fact necessary to support the board's adjudication were supported by substantial evidence.


OUTCOME: The order upholding one condition and reversing two conditions placed upon dredging permits issued by the Pennsylvania Department of Environmental Resources to appellant gravel companies was affirmed because the department had the authority to regulate dredging activities and the findings of fact were supported by substantial evidence.

Kaufman & Broad, Inc. v. Board of Supervisors


No. 976 C.D. 1974


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


20 Pa. Commw. 116; 340 A.2d 909; 1975 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1075


April 1, 1975, Argued  
July 2, 1975, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Chester County in case of Kaufman and Broad, Inc. v. Board of Supervisors of West Whiteland Township, No. 226 March Term, 1974.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property owner sought review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Chester County (Pennsylvania), which sustained appellee board of supervisors' denial of appellant's request for a curative amendment to a township zoning ordinance.


OVERVIEW: Appellant property owner filed a request for a curative amendment to a township zoning ordinance and attached a proposed planned residential development (PRD), which consisted of, inter alia, four-plex units and condominiums. Appellee board of supervisors refused to accept either the plans or the curative amendment. The lower court affirmed the decision. The court reversed the order, holding that the PRD request was not the proper subject matter of a curative amendment submission, under § 1004 of the Municipalities Planning Code (MPD), 53 P.S. § 11004(1)(b), as PRD provisions were not mandated by MPC and no one could compel their enforcement. The case was remanded to appellee for a determination of whether a four-plex unit was an excluded use under the ordinance. Because appellant's curative amendment submission was overbroad, the trial court was instructed to exercise its broad supervisory powers of review, as the township could not be compelled to pass appellant's submission and would not be able to enact a reasonable and proper curative amendment in a reasonable time. The court provided detailed remand guidelines.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order, holding that appellant property owner's proposed planned residential development (PRD) was not the proper subject of a curative amendment submission, as the Municipalities Planning Code did not mandate PRD provisions and no one was charged with enforcing PRDs. The case was remanded, as appellant's submission was overbroad and the township could not enact a curative amendment in a reasonable time.

Kadash v. Williamsport


No. 1256 C.D. 1974


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


19 Pa. Commw. 643; 340 A.2d 617; 1975 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1053


March 7, 1975, Argued  
June 13, 1975, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Lycoming County in case of City of Williamsport v. George Kadash, No. 73-10,419.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of an order from the Court of Common Pleas of Lycoming County (Pennsylvania) finding him guilty of violating a city ordinance prohibiting nuisances, including the storage of junk cars.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of violating a city ordinance prohibiting nuisances, including the storage of junk cars. Appellant sought review of that conviction, admitting that he stored the autos openly on his property in violation of the ordinance, but challenging the sufficiency of the facts to prove the conviction as well as the authority of the city to enact the ordinance. The court found that while the city was authorized to enact such an ordinance, the ordinance, in order to ensure that it was a constitutional exercise of police power, was required to be interpreted to prohibit the storage of junk cars in such a manner as to create a nuisance. Consistent with that interpretation, the court found that the city had the burden of proving that the stored cars created a nuisance and that the city had failed to carry that burden.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment and conviction, finding that the city had failed to carry its burden of proving that appellant had violated the ordinance prohibiting the storage of junk cars in such a manner as to create a nuisance.

Columbia Gas Transmission Corp. v. Commonwealth

OVERVIEW: Appellant taxpayer was a foreign corporation. The court determined that there should have been apportionment of the tax levied on appellee's natural gas that moved through and out of the commonwealth. The court sustained the appeal to the extent of the apportionment issue.


OUTCOME: The court sustained the appeal, having determined that although appellant taxpayer's constitutional arguments were without merit, appellee revenue department should have apportioned the amount of capital attributable to natural gas flowing through and out of the commonwealth.

School Dist. v. W. T. Grant Corp.


Nos. 1763, 1764 and 1765 C.D. 1973


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


19 Pa. Commw. 402; 339 A.2d 628; 1975 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1019


March 31, 1975, Argued  
June 2, 1975, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Reargument Filed and Denied.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Orders of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County in cases of The School District of Philadelphia v. W.T. Grant Corporation, No. 2038 January Term 1971; the School District of Philadelphia v. The Southland Corporation, No. 2608 May Term 1970; and The School District of Philadelphia v. Penn Fruit Company, No. 3826 October Term 1970.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city school district sought review of the orders of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which granted motions for summary judgment in favor of appellees interstate commerce corporations in regard to tax assessments by appellant. Appellant alleged that appellees miscalculated their taxes under 24 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 584.3 by applying a net income limitation.


OVERVIEW: Appellants interstate commerce corporations were subject to taxation by appellant city school district but objected to appellant's assessment of delinquencies in their payments. The lower court granted appellees' motions for summary judgment and allowed appellees to calculate their taxes based on an interpretation of 24 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 584.3, which allowed appellees to apply a net income limitation to their tax allocation. On appeal, the court analyzed the statute for uniformity and found that the net income limitation violated uniformity because it allowed a ceiling on tax liability of a gross receipts tax to be measured by net income. The court ruled that the statute was unconstitutional, but that removing the severable provision from the statute regarding the net income limitation would cure the defect.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the lower court that granted summary judgment in favor of appellees interstate commerce corporations. The court granted summary judgment in favor of appellant city school district. The court also declared unconstitutional a provision of state tax law that included a tax ceiling of 2 percent of net income, because it violated uniform assessment of taxes.

Gwinn v. Kane


No. 5 Tr. Dkt. 1975


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


19 Pa. Commw. 243; 339 A.2d 838; 1975 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 997

May 27, 1975, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Original jurisdiction in case of Richard H. Gwinn v. Robert P. Kane, Walter M. Phillips, Jr., Mark A. Klugheit and Ronald G. Cole.



DISPOSITION: Plaintiff's motion denied. Defendants' motion granted. Judgment for defendants.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff moved for summary judgment in an action in quo warranto seeking to void an indictment charging him with perjury and false swearing. Plaintiff challenged the office held by defendant deputy attorney general and special prosecutor and the role played by his subordinates, also defendants in the action, in the investigation and presentment leading to plaintiff's indictment and the role they were expected to play in plaintiff's trial.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was appointed deputy attorney general and special prosecutor to work with a grand jury to investigate and prosecute political and police corruption. Plaintiff, a president of a milk company, testified before the grand jury regarding contracts between his milk company and the city. The grand jury issued a presentment and approved the indictment of plaintiff for perjury and false swearing. Plaintiff brought an action in quo warranto to challenge the legality of the office held by defendant deputy attorney general and his actions, along with the actions of his subordinates, under the authority of that office. Both parties moved for summary judgment. The court held that plaintiff had standing to bring the action in quo warranto as an individual with a special interest in testing the legality of the office beyond that of the general public. The court concluded that defendant was appointed to a lawful office for a proper and lawful purpose and that he was acting within the scope and authority of that office in investigating and prosecuting charges against plaintiff. Summary judgment was entered in favor of defendants and against plaintiff.


OUTCOME: The court denied plaintiff's motion for summary judgment and granted defendants' motion for summary judgment, finding that defendant deputy attorney general was appointed to a lawful office for a proper and lawful purpose and that defendant was acting within the scope of his authority in his actions leading to a presentment against and indictment of plaintiff.

Ralph W. Connelly, Inc. v. Board of Supervisors


No. 1519 C.D. 1974


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


19 Pa. Commw. 110; 340 A.2d 597; 1975 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 979


April 3, 1975, Argued  
May 16, 1975, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Chester County in case of In Re: Appeal of Ralph W. Connelly, Inc., No. 248 July Term, 1973.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant township challenged an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Chester County (Pennsylvania), which declared a section of the township ordinance to be unconstitutional as applied to appellee landowner's property under 53 P.S. § 11004, and which stated that the lower court would retain jurisdiction for the purpose of issuing appropriate supplementary orders.


OVERVIEW: Appellee landowner filed a challenge to a local zoning ordinance of appellant township that required a minimum lot area of three acres for a single-family dwelling under the provisions of the Municipalities Planning Code Act, 53 P.S. § 11004. The court reversed the holding that the zoning ordinance was unconstitutional because appellee failed to follow the mandatory requirements of 53 P.S. § 11004(2)(c), that his request for relief had to be accompanied by plans and other materials describing the proposed use or development. Therefore, his request was procedurally defective. Appellee's mere statement of intent to develop at some time in the future was not sufficient to give him standing under 53 P.S. § 11004. The court also noted that even if appellee had followed the proper procedures, his brief testimony at the hearing below was insufficient to satisfy his burden of proving that the present zoning of his property was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order which had declared a section of the township ordinance to be unconstitutional as applied to appellee landowner's property because appellee failed to follow the procedural requirements for challenging the constitutionality of the ordinance.

737 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Penn Federal Sav. & Loan Asso. v. Tax Review Board


No. 1450 C.D. 1973


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


18 Pa. Commw. 530; 336 A.2d 907; 1975 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1377


April 3, 1975, Argued  
April 29, 1975, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County in case of Penn Federal Savings and Loan Association of Philadelphia v. Tax Review Board of the City of Philadelphia, No. 460 July Term, 1973.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant bank challenged a decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia (Pennsylvania) which affirmed a determination of the city's tax review board denying appellant a refund for property taxes paid in error by appellant on behalf of its mortgagor, the taxpayer.


OVERVIEW: Appellant bank, following its standard procedure to pay taxes on real estate on which it held the mortgage and to charge the mortgagor's account, requested from the city the amount of taxes due on mortgagor's property, but erroneously supplied the city with mortgagor's business address rather than the address of the mortgaged property. The city provided the information, and appellant paid the taxes. Appellant's request for a refund was denied by appellee Tax Review Board of the City of Philadelphia, and the lower court affirmed. The court affirmed. Appellant had shown no mistake of fact permitting a refund under Philadelphia, Pa., Code of Municipal Ordinances § 19-1703(1)(a). The taxes paid were properly due to appellant. No mistake was made as to the tax, its amount, or the property on which it was paid. Instead, appellant's mistake was its own authority from its mortgagor, the taxpayer, to pay the tax. Evidence that the proper taxpayer-corporation might have been bankrupt at the time appellant discovered its mistake did not affect this determination, since there was no evidence that the corporation was in bankruptcy at the time the tax was paid.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's order affirming a determination of appellee Tax Review Board of the City of Philadelphia which denied a property tax refund to appellant bank, because no mistake of fact or law existed as to whether the tax was properly due to the city, but only as to appellant's authority from the taxpayer to pay the tax.

 

738 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. National Federation of Blind


No. 1129 C.D. 1974


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


18 Pa. Commw. 291; 335 A.2d 832; 1975 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 897


January 8, 1975, Argued  
April 10, 1975, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Original jurisdiction in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. National Federation of the Blind and American Brotherhood for the Blind, Inc.



DISPOSITION: Preliminary objections overruled.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, two organizations representing blind persons, raised preliminary objections challenging the personal jurisdiction of the trial court (Pennsylvania), as well as other objections, in a suit filed by plaintiff, the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, seeking to enjoin defendants from further solicitations of residents until they complied with the registration requirements of 10 P.S. § 160-3.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff, the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, sought to enjoin defendants, organizations representing blind persons, from conducting solicitations of Pennsylvania residents until defendants complied with the registration requirements of 10 P.S. § 160-3. Defendants raised preliminary objections challenging the personal jurisdiction of the court as violating their due process rights under U.S. Const. amend. XIV, as well as other objections. The court overruled the objections. It first held that mailed solicitations by an out-of-state charity were, for purposes of the minimal contacts test, comparable to a much greater activity by a commercial enterprise, and that defendants established minimal contacts by mailing of solicitations to residents. The court held that the method of service provided in 10 P.S. § 160-14 did not impinge upon defendants' due process rights, that the court's subject matter jurisdiction did not unconstitutionally invade the domain of the federal government in matters involving the use of the mails, and that notice and a hearing were not conditions precedent to commencement of a § 160-14 action based on failure to file a registration statement.


OUTCOME: The court overruled defendants' preliminary objections, holding that they established minimal contacts by mailing of solicitations to residents, that the method of service did not impinge upon their due process rights, that the court's subject matter jurisdiction did not invade the domain of the federal government in matters involving use of the mails, and that due notice and hearing were not conditions precedent to commencement of the action.

Commonwealth, Dep't of Environmental Resources v. Commonwealth, Public Utility Com.


Nos. 1252, 1259 and 1273 C.D. 1974


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


18 Pa. Commw. 558; 335 A.2d 860; 1975 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 946


February 3, 1975, Argued  
April 10, 1975, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals from the Orders of the Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission in cases of In Re: Applications of The Potomac Edison Company of Pennsylvania, Docket Nos. 97560 and 97562.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania Department of Environmental Resources and affected landowners, sought review of two final orders of appellee Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission which approved applications and granted certificates of public convenience and necessity to an electric company for the right to proceed in eminent domain for an electric transmission line right-of-way pursuant to 15 P.S. § 3272.


OVERVIEW: Appellee Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission granted certificates of public convenience and necessity to an electric company for the right to proceed in eminent domain for an electric transmission line right-of-way pursuant to 15 P.S. § 3272. The right-of-way passed near a historic property, and appellants, the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania Department of Environmental Resources and affected landowners, challenged the decision. The court affirmed appellee's conclusion that the route selected by the electric company was reasonable and ruled that the condemnation of the easement required was in accord with the company's right to appropriate private real estate under the provisions of 15 P.S. § 3272. The court rejected appellants' argument that appellee had failed to evaluate the electric company's application under Pa. Const. art. I, § 27, which required the Commonwealth to conserve and maintain the Commonwealth's natural and historic resources. The court applied its threefold standard for review under Pa. Const. art. I, § 27 to the record and affirmed the orders of appellee.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the orders of appellee Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission allowing an electric company to proceed in eminent domain for an electric transmission line right-of-way. The court would not set aside the selection of a right-of-way unless the powers conferred upon the public utility were wantonly, capriciously, or arbitrarily exercised.

Pennsylvania Barber Schools, Inc. v. Alfano


No. 1404 C.D. 1974


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


18 Pa. Commw. 54; 333 A.2d 834; 1975 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 860


March 5, 1975, Argued  
March 20, 1975, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Original jurisdiction in case of Pennsylvania Barber Schools, Inc. v. Anthony Alfano, Thomas C. Bigler, Stanley O. Thomas and Robert V. Vavro, individually and acting as State Board of Barber Examiners, c/o Department of Professional and Occupational Affairs.



DISPOSITION: Preliminary objections sustained. Petition dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff association of barber schools filed a petition for declaratory judgment, seeking to have the court declare 63 P.S. § 553(a), requiring plaintiff's students to serve a substantial apprenticeship, unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff association of barber schools filed a petition for declaratory judgment, seeking to have 63 P.S. § 553(a) declared unconstitutional because it required plaintiff's students to serve a substantial apprenticeship, whereas students who attended schools of cosmetology were not so required. The court sustained defendants' preliminary objections, which raised the question of whether plaintiff had standing to bring the action. The court dismissed plaintiff's petition for declaratory judgment, holding that plaintiff lacked standing to bring the action because the individuals whose rights were purportedly being infringed were the students required to undertake the apprenticeship. The court found that the fact that plaintiff would be generally adversely affected did not in itself put plaintiff in the class authorized to seek a declaratory judgment. The court concluded that plaintiff had no direct interest, but only the collateral interest of the students.


OUTCOME: The court sustained defendants' preliminary objections and dismissed the petition of plaintiff association of barber schools for declaratory judgment. The court held that plaintiff lacked standing to bring the action, as it had no direct interest in the action where its students' rights were purportedly being infringed.

Commonwealth by Packel v. Pennsylvania Interscholastic Athletic Asso.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff state filed a motion for summary judgment in its suit against defendant interscholastic athletic association challenging the constitutionality of defendant's by-law under the equal protection clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV and Pa. Const. art. I, § 28.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff state initiated suit against defendant interscholastic athletic association, challenging the constitutionality of defendant's by-law that allegedly denied to female student athletes the same opportunities available to males to practice for and compete in interscholastic sports, in violation of the equal protection clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV and Pa. Const. art. I, § 28. Defendant answered and plaintiff filed a motion for summary judgment. The court granted plaintiff's motion and declared the by-law unconstitutional under Pa. The court found that defendant's justifications for the by-law could not sustain its legality, as the exclusion of girls solely because of sex without regard to relevant qualifications denied equality under the law.


OUTCOME: The court granted plaintiff state's motion for summary judgment in its suit against defendant interscholastic athletic association challenging the constitutionality of defendant's by-law, as exclusion of girls from practicing and competing with boys in interscholastic athletics solely because of sex without regard to relevant qualifications denied equality under the law.

Rochez Bros., Inc. v. Commonwealth, Dep't of Environmental Resources


No. 506 C.D. 1974


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


18 Pa. Commw. 137; 334 A.2d 790; 1975 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 874


January 6, 1975, Argued  
March 19, 1975, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Environmental Hearing Board in case of In the Matter of: Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, Department of Environmental Resources v. Rouchez Brothers, Inc., d/b/a Lucerne Coke Company, Docket No. 73-367-B.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant coal company sought review of an order, issued by the Environmental Hearing Board (Pennsylvania), affirming the denial by appellee, Pennsylvania Department of Environmental Resources, of appellant's application to reactivate its beehive coke ovens.


OVERVIEW: When appellee, Pennsylvania Department of Environmental Resources, denied appellant coal company's application for permission to reactivate beehive coke ovens, appellant sought review by the Environmental Hearing Board. After the board sustained appellee's denial, appellant sought judicial review. The court affirmed, finding no error of law by the board in ruling that appellee properly disapproved the application, because it did not comply with statutory requirements found in the Air Pollution Control Act, 35 P.S. § 4001 et seq. (APCA). It upheld the board's holding that none of appellant's constitutional rights were violated, despite the fact that it was impossible for appellant to legally utilize the chosen processing method, because 35 P.S. § 4006.1, as applied to appellant, was a reasonable exercise of the commonwealth's police power. The court found that appellant had no right, constitutional or otherwise, to emit harmful pollutants in violation of the APCA; and that adverse economic impact might result from denial of appellant's application did not provide a basis on which appellee or the board could disregard the provisions of the APCA, and related regulations.


OUTCOME: The court upheld an Environmental Hearing Board order, approving a ruling by appellee Department of Environmental Resources, denying appellant coal company's application to reactivate certain coal ovens, finding that appellee had no power to exempt appellant from compliance with the statute; and that the fact that appellant's ovens could not be operated in compliance with those statutes did not violate appellant's constitutional rights.

Pennsylvania State Harness Racing Com. v. Dancer


No. 1022 C.D. 1974


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


16 Pa. Commw. 575; 333 A.2d 196; 1975 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 727


February 7, 1975, Argued  
February 27, 1975, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Pennsylvania State Harness Racing Commission in case of Ronald S. Dancer v. Pennsylvania State Harness Racing Commission, No. P.L. 17527.



DISPOSITION: Appeal dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant harness racing driver sought review of the order from the Pennsylvania State Harness Racing Commission, which affirmed the action of a starter and presiding judge of a race track that appellant be suspended for three days for violating Pa. State Harness Racing Comm'n Rule 16, § 1(k)(7) by failing to come up into position.


OVERVIEW: Appellant harness racing driver was racing a dangerous horse, which his father said could be controlled only by keeping him out of the starting gate when the race began. Race track officials warned appellant that doing so would not be accepted, but appellant kept doing it. The court affirmed the decision of appellee. The court held that Pa. State Harness Racing Comm'n Rule 16, § 1(k)(7), which required a driver to come up into position, was not unconstitutionally vague, because a driver of ordinary intelligence could understand what was meant. The court also held that appellee's decision was amply supported by the evidence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of appellee Pennsylvania State Harness Racing Commission to affirm the suspension handed down by the race track's starter and presiding judge for failure to come up into position. The court held that the rule requiring drivers to come up into position was not unconstitutionally vague, as a driver of ordinary intelligence would understand what was meant.

 

744 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Hess v. Upper Oxford Township


No. 126 C.D. 1974


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


17 Pa. Commw. 399; 332 A.2d 836; 1975 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 806


October 7, 1974, Argued  
February 11, 1975, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Chester County in case of Clay C. Hess and Ivan W. Hess v. Upper Oxford Township, No. 37 July Term, 1973.



DISPOSITION: Remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landowners sought review of the decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Chester County (Pennsylvania), that dismissed the appeal of the denial of their application for a curative amendment to the Upper Oxford Township, Penn. Ordinances, art. IV, filed pursuant to 53 P.S. § 10609.1, which requested that the ordinance be amended to permit smaller minimum lots with a smaller lot width at the building line.


OVERVIEW: Appellant landowners sought review when their appeal of the denial of their application for a curative amendment to the Upper Oxford Township, Penn. Ordinances, art. IV was dismissed. The court remanded the case ordering the lower court to make appropriate findings of fact after concluding that it was unable to properly review the matter because the record was insufficient. Appellants wanted an amendment to enable them to develop their tract of property with lot sizes less than the two acre minimum mandated by the ordinance. Because the administrative body did not make a decision within 30 days after the hearing was held, the amendment request was deemed denied pursuant to 53 P.S. § 11004(4)(iii). The lower court dismissed the appeal without hearing additional evidence. The appellate court found that it was unable to consider appellants' challenges because the record was lacking findings of fact. It held, therefore, that irrespective of the fact that 53 P.S. § 11010 did not require findings of fact when reviewing the denial of a curative amendment, it was the duty of the lower court to make appropriate findings of fact.


OUTCOME: The court remanded the case for the lower court to enter findings of fact, and if necessary, to take additional evidence in order to make appropriate findings. Although not required by statute, it was the duty of the lower court to make appropriate findings of fact when reviewing the denial of a curative amendment in order to enable a meaningful review on appeal.

Ellick v. Board of Supervisors


No. 201 C.D. 1974


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


17 Pa. Commw. 404; 333 A.2d 239; 1975 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 807


October 9, 1974, Argued  
February 11, 1975, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County in case of Marvin D. Ellick v. Board of Supervisors of Worcester Township, No. 73-13082.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landowner sought relief from the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County (Pennsylvania), which affirmed the rejection by the Worcester Township Board of Supervisors of appellant's challenge to the validity of the zoning ordinance of appellee township and request for curative amendment. Appellant alleged that the ordinance improperly prohibited all townhouses.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was the owner of a 40-acre tract of land located in appellee township. Appellant's land was currently zoned agricultural, which limited non-agricultural uses to lots of 60,000 square feet. Appellant desired to build 280 townhouses on his property, and filed a challenge with appellee's board of supervisors pursuant to 53 Pa. Stat. § 11004(1)(b), alleging that townhouses for sale were excluded in every portion of appellee township. The board dismissed appellant's appeal, and the trial court affirmed. The court reversed, holding that the ordinance in question, on its face, totally prohibited townhouses, a legitimate and needed residential use and that, therefore, it was invalid. The court remanded the matter to the trial court for the purpose of determining whether the plans and other materials submitted by appellant should be ordered approved in whole or in part.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order, holding that the subject zoning ordinance improperly prohibited all townhouses, a legitimate residential use. The court remanded the matter for a determination of whether appellant landowner's plans and other materials should be ordered approved in whole or in part.

 

746 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Knauer v. Commonwealth


No. 8 Tr. Dkt. 1973


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


17 Pa. Commw. 360; 332 A.2d 589; 1975 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 799; 88 L.R.R.M. 2814


January 9, 1975, Argued  
February 10, 1975, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Alton Glenson Knauer, No. 633 June Term, 1971.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employee sought review of the decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County (Pennsylvania), that dismissed his appeal from an order finding him guilty of violating an ordinance regulating picketing activities for allegedly hitting someone on the head while picketing in front of his employer's place of business.


OVERVIEW: Appellant employee sought review when he was convicted of violating an ordinance regulating picketing activities for committing assault and battery against another employee while engaged in picketing in front of his employer's place of business. He challenged the denial of his appeal. The court reversed the order denying his appeal and his judgment, concluding that the township exceeded its authority in attempting to regulate picketing activities. The court reasoned that the First Class Township Code, 53 P.S. § 55101 et seq., granted townships the power to provide regulations on many subjects, but did not mention picketing. It concluded that the township was without the authority to regulate picketing, and that the ordinance was unauthorized. Further, since appellant was actually convicted of assault and battery, the court found that the township again exceeded its authority by attempting to punish an activity punishable by Act of Assembly, 18 P.S. § 4406, as a violation of a township ordinance.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order dismissing the appeal and the judgment against appellant employee, concluding that the township's powers were limited, that it could not exercise powers exceeding those that it possessed, that the subject ordinance was invalid because it exceeded the powers granted to the township by the legislature, and that appellant could not be found to have violated an unauthorized ordinance.

748 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Tarentum v. Sadecky


No. 160 C.D. 1974


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


16 Pa. Commw. 163; 329 A.2d 328; 1974 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 608


November 8, 1974, Argued  
December 17, 1974, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Stephen M. Sadecky, No. SA 587 of 1973.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Venire facias de novo ordered.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant borough sought review of an order from the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which dismissed a charge for violating 53 P.S. § 46202 as against appellee homeowner and declared the ordinance unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Appellee homeowner was charged generally with violating appellant borough's ordinance that made it unlawful for any person to accumulate junked, abandoned or discarded vehicles, trailers, boats, appliances, or household furnishings on private or public property. Appellee enacted the ordinance pursuant to the power it was given by the Borough Code, 53 P.S. § 46202. Appellee appeared before a district justice, was found guilty, and ordered to pay a fine and costs. At a de novo hearing in the trial court, the judge remarked that he had just read the ordinance, and that it was unconstitutional. The hearing was terminated by the judge's expressed desire that the borough not continue because to do so would have been useless. The judge thereupon adjudged appellee not guilty. On appeal, judgment was reversed, and a venire facias de novo was awarded. The court held that legislative enactments were presumed to be constitutional, and that the courts could not substitute their judgment for that of the legislature. The court held that even if parts of the ordinance were invalid, the judge could not have deemed the entire ordinance unconstitutional after reading it only one time.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment for appellant, and a venire facias de novo was awarded because while some portions of the ordinance in question were invalid, other portions were valid.

 

749 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Thayer v. Lower Milford Township


No. 343 C.D. 1974


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


16 Pa. Commw. 124; 343 A.2d 92; 1974 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 602


October 11, 1974, Argued  
December 12, 1974, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Lehigh County in case of William H. Thayer and Josephine Thayer v. Lower Milford Township and Lower Milford Township Zoning Hearing Board, No. 410 June Term, 1973.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landowners sought review of an order of the Common Court of Pleas, Lehigh County (Pennsylvania), which dismissed their appeal of the decision of appellee zoning hearing board that denied appellants a variance to permit certain portions of their land for use as a tractor-trailer repair facility.


OVERVIEW: Appellant landowners sought a variance to use certain portions of their land as a tractor-trailer repair facility in a residential district. Appellants claimed that prior to the purchase of the property, the former landowners operated an automobile repair shop. Appellee zoning hearing board (board) denied appellant's variance. The lower court affirmed. On appeal, the court likewise affirmed. The court held that there was no basis for the court to grant the relief requested because the zoning ordinance that controlled the use of appellants' land was in effect for several years prior to their purchase. The court also held that the operation of a trailer repair compound was not in the public interest in the area. The court further held that appellants were entitled to continue the nonconforming use that was being conducted on the property at the time the ordinance was enacted. However, appellants were entitled to only reasonable expansion, the extent of which was to be determined by the board, and, as such, it was within the board's discretion to deny appellants' variance.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's dismissal of appellant landowners' appeal. The court held that the decision of appellee zoning hearing board was proper because the zoning ordinance that controlled the use of appellants' land was in effect at the time of purchase, and although appellants were entitled to continue nonconforming use, it was within the board's discretion to reasonably limit the expansion of the nonconforming use.

School Dist. v. Damico, Inc.

15 Pa. Commw. 558; 328 A.2d 190; 1974 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 775


October 7, 1974, Argued  
November 14, 1974, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County in case of Damico, Inc. v. The School District of Philadelphia and Thomas W. Rogers, No. 2104 September Term, 1971.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, school district and associated individual, sought review of a decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which granted a permanent injunction that enjoined the collection of a tax, pursuant to the Philadelphia Business Use and Occupancy Tax, from appellee taxpayer.


OVERVIEW: Appellee taxpayer leased and operated a parking garage in a portion of a building where office space is also located. Appellants, school district and associated individual, sought review of a decision that enjoined the collection of a tax pursuant to the Philadelphia Business Use and Occupancy Tax (tax) from appellee. The court held that the imposition the tax which was to a substantial degree unequal in its operation or effect upon similar kinds of business or property, or upon persons in the same classification, was prohibited.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed trial court's injunction that prohibited the collection of a business use tax from appellee taxpayer because the tax violated the state constitution's requirement that such a tax be uniform.

Wildman Arms, Inc. v. Zoning Hearing Bd.


No. 1317 C.D. 1973


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


15 Pa. Commw. 569; 328 A.2d 528; 1974 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 777


October 9, 1974, Argued  
November 12, 1974, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County in case of Wildman Arms, Inc. of Swarthmore v. The Zoning Hearing Board of the Borough of Swarthmore, No. 107 of 1973.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant owner of an apartment-hotel complex sought review of an order from the Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County (Pennsylvania), which affirmed an order by appellee zoning hearing board that denied appellant's request for a permit to build a directional sign.


OVERVIEW: Appellant owner of an apartment-hotel complex applied to the building inspector for a permit to install and maintain a sign and the building inspector refused the requested permit. Appellant sought review from appellee zoning hearing board. Appellee conducted a public hearing, and then filed its report that contained findings of fact and conclusions of law, which dismissed appellant's appeal. The trial court affirmed appellee's decision. On appeal, the court affirmed for appellee. The court held that even if the entire zoning ordinance was unconstitutional, in that it made no provision for the erection, maintenance, and/or display of directional signs within a residence or apartment house district and/or within the entire borough, the municipality had produced testimony that evidenced at least two public interests sought to be protected, and both of these interests bore a substantial relationship to the public health, safety, morals, and general welfare of the municipality. The court affirmed for appellee because legislation was presumed lawful unless proven unlawful by the person challenging it.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court for appellee zoning hearing board because the ordinances were valid in that they regulated in the usual and customary manner where it was desired to exclude commercial activity in a residentially zoned area and permitted only the announcement of the presence of public service, professional, institutional, and non profit uses and bore a substantial relationship to the public health, safety, and morals.

 

753 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Ramey Borough v. Commonwealth, Dep't of Environmental Resources


No. 89 C.D. 1974


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


15 Pa. Commw. 601; 327 A.2d 647; 1974 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 783


September 6, 1974, Argued  
October 29, 1974, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Environmental Hearing Board in case of In the Matter of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, Department of Environmental Resources v. Ramey Borough, No. 73-108.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant borough sought review of the order of the Environmental Hearing Board (Pennsylvania), which affirmed appellee Department of Environmental Resources' order directing appellant to plan, design, construct, and operate a sewage treatment facility.


OVERVIEW: Appellant borough was ordered by the environmental hearing board (board) to plan, design, construct, and operate a sewage treatment facility. Appellant alleged that appellee Department of Environmental Resources failed to submit evidence from which the board could make a finding that appellant was discharging untreated sewage into the streams. The court affirmed the order, holding that while the record did not disclose evidence introduced by appellee that untreated sewage was being discharged, the board's conclusion was properly based on evidence presented by appellant itself. The court held that appellant's own witness testified that 90 homes were connected to the sewer system that discharged untreated sewage and, having introduced such uncontradicted evidence through its own witness, appellant could not assert on appeal that the record was devoid of evidence to support the conclusion that the streams were being polluted. The court further held that the construction financing and operating cost projections were only estimates and that a constitutional challenge could not be sustained on the basis of speculation as to future events.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order directing appellant borough to plan, design, construct, and operate a sewage treatment facility. The court held that the environmental hearing board's decision was properly based on evidence presented by appellant borough that 90 houses were connected to a sewer system that discharged untreated water into streams.

 

754 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Federal Reserve Bank v. Pennsylvania Public Utility Com.


No. 1743 C.D. 1973


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


15 Pa. Commw. 360; 326 A.2d 643; 1974 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 741


September 9, 1974, Argued  
October 22, 1974, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission in cases of In Re: Application of American Courier Corporation for a certificate of public convenience evidencing the Commission's approval of the right and privilege of operating motor vehicles as a common carrier for the transportation of property, No. A.80419, Folder No. F.2, and In Re: Application of Protective Motor Service Company for a certificate of public convenience evidencing the Commission's approval of the right and privilege of operating motor vehicles as a common carrier for the transportation of property, No. A 26177, Folder No. F.3.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant banks sought review from an order of appellee Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission, which granted two applications that requested conversions of contract carrier authority to that of a Class D common carrier.


OVERVIEW: Appellant banks challenged an order of appellees Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission (commission), which granted two applications that requested conversions of contract carrier authority to that of a Class D common carrier. The court concluded that the particularized needs of customers were only one element of the good-cause standard of the Public Utility Law, 66 P.S. § 1305. Although appellants had demonstrated a particular need for them to utilize the services of the carriers, it did not follow that this automatically required the commission to allow dual carrier status. This was especially true where, as here, the evidence indicated that the services of the applicants as common carriers would be equal or more advantageous than their services as contract carriers in fulfilling the particular needs of the bank shippers. The court held that the commission's action was not arbitrary, capricious, or unreasonable. Accordingly, the court affirmed the order.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order against appellant banks and in favor of appellees Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission (commission) regarding applications requesting conversions of contract carrier authority. The court held that the particularized needs of customers were only one element of the good-cause standard of the Public Utility Law, and that the commission's action was not arbitrary, capricious, or unreasonable.

Vance v. Kassab


No. 23 C.D. 1974


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


15 Pa. Commw. 328; 325 A.2d 924; 1974 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 734


September 5, 1974, Argued  
October 7, 1974, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Original jurisdiction in case of Charles D. Vance and Patricia H. Vance, his wife, v. Jacob G. Kassab, Secretary of the Department of Transportation, Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, and H.J. Williams Company.



DISPOSITION: Complaint dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, state secretary of transportation and contractor, filed demurrers to a complaint executed by plaintiff landowners seeking an order restraining defendants from constructing a pipe to drain water from a township road across plaintiffs' property into a creek.


OVERVIEW: Defendant state secretary of transportation hired defendant contractor for a project that included moving a road and installing a drainage pipe in order to drain water from the road across plaintiff landowners' property into a creek. Plaintiffs filed suit for violations of The Clean Streams Law, 35 P.S. § 691.401, and Pa. Const. art. I, § 27. Defendants filed demurrers based on immunity from suit, but the court dismissed the complaint on other grounds. The court held that plaintiffs failed to state a cause of action within equity's jurisdiction. 36 P.S. § 670-417 allowed the construction of facilities for carrying surface waters from the public roads. The court stated that if defendants discharged water onto plaintiffs' land in the manner described by the complaint, then plaintiffs' remedy was through The Eminent Domain Code, 26 P.S. § 1-201 et seq., and the court had no jurisdiction to inquire as to the validity of the possible taking that had not yet occurred.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed plaintiff landowners' complaint requesting that defendants, state secretary of transportation and contractor, be restrained from constructing a drainage pipe across plaintiffs' land because plaintiffs failed to state a cause of action within equity's jurisdiction.

Hyatt and the Mary D. Newmyer Estate, No. 471 Civil Docket 1971.

The court found that the lower court had jurisdiction to determine the annexation issue and the legal question of whether the annexation was procedurally defective. The court held that appellant township was not entitled to reopen the prior decree because appeal had not been taken and relitigation was barred by res judicata.

Pennsylvania Gas & Water Co. v. Kassab


No. 1073 C.D. 1973


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


14 Pa. Commw. 564; 322 A.2d 775; 1974 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 864


June 4, 1974, Argued  
July 23, 1974, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Original jurisdiction in case of Pennsylvania Gas and Water Company, Stegmaier Brewing Company, John Watkins and Francis Zirnhelt v. Jacob Kassab, Grace M. Sloan and Kaminski Brothers, Inc.



DISPOSITION: Motion denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants state officials filed a motion for judgment on the pleadings in a lawsuit filed by plaintiffs, water company and its customers, that sought an order to restrain defendants from performing construction work on a highway that would allegedly pollute a reservoir owned by plaintiff water company.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff water company owned a reservoir. Plaintiffs, water company and its customers, filed suit against defendant state officials seeking to restrain defendants from performing construction work on a highway. Plaintiffs alleged that the construction work would cause runoff into the reservoir which would pollute the reservoir and reduce its storage capacity. Plaintiffs alleged that the pollution wold violate plaintiffs' constitutional rights to have pure water. Pa. Const. art. I, § 27. The court denied defendants' motion for judgment of the pleadings. The court discussed the standards for ruling on motions for judgment on the pleadings and recognized that a party moving for judgment of the pleadings admits for the purposes of the motion all of the allegations of the opposing party. The court rejected defendants' contention that plaintiff's had an adequate remedy at law because plaintiffs denied that eminent domain gave them an adequate remedy at law since they could not obtain a restraining order in an eminent domain proceeding. The court also found that defendants were not immune from a suit that sought to restrain them from unconstitutional or illegal action.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant state officials motion for judgment on the pleadings because defendants did not establish that plaintiffs, water company and its customers, had an adequate remedy at law or that defendants were immune from suit.

Leap v. Commonwealth, Dep't of Labor & Industry


No. 974 C.D. 1973


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


14 Pa. Commw. 324; 322 A.2d 398; 1974 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 826


January 7, 1974, Argued  
July 10, 1974, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargued June 5, 1974.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Order of the Department of Labor and Industry in case of In Re: Application of Don T. Leap, Case No. 680-BF-S.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant business owner sought review of an order from appellee Department of Labor and Industry (Pennsylvania), which denied appellant's application for a permit to operate two steam locomotives.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed the order of appellee Department of Labor and Industry which denied appellant business owner a permit to operate two steam locomotives, because the boilers had not been inspected during construction as required under the Boiler Act (Act), 35 P.S. § 1304. The court found that the denial of appellant's permit was proper because the Act applied to all boilers that were destined for use within the state and required the shop inspection of such boilers. The court held that although the locomotives had been built in Germany, there was still a great difficulty in determining the quality of the boiler after it was built and that the Act was intended to protect persons and property from unsafe boilers. The court concluded that appellee had the authority to withhold appellant's permits because the boilers had not been shop inspected as required under the Act.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of appellant business owner's permits to operate two steam locomotives because it found that appellee Department of Labor and Industry was authorized to withhold the permits as the boilers had not been shop inspected as required under statute.

Meth v. Commonwealth, State Real Estate Com.


Nos. 1180 and 1182 C.D. 1973


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


14 Pa. Commw. 203; 321 A.2d 221; 1974 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 806


February 5, 1974, Argued  
June 20, 1974, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals from the Order of the State Real Estate Commission in case of In the Matter of the Suspension or Revocation of the License to Practice as a Real Estate Broker, No. 11574 issued August 2, 1948, to Charles Meth, and No. 16515 issued May 4, 1956, to Ralph Myers, Case No. 2128.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant real estate brokers sought review of an order of appellee State Real Estate Commission (Pennsylvania), which revoked appellants' real estate broker's licenses after finding appellants violated § 11(b) of the Real Estate Brokers License Act of 1929, 63 P.S. § 441(b).


OVERVIEW: Appellee State Real Estate Commission revoked the real estate broker's licenses of appellant real estate brokers after they entered pleas of nolo contendere to three counts of submitting a false statement involving the Federal Housing Administration in violation of 18 U.S.C.S. § 1001 in the federal court. On appeal, the court affirmed appellee's decision. Appellees' real estate broker's licenses were properly revoked pursuant to § 11(b) of the Real Estate Brokers License Act of 1929, 63 P.S. § 441(b), which provided that a license shall be revoked if a licensee entered a plea of nolo contendere in a court of competent jurisdiction in the state, or any other state, to an offense involving obligations insured by the United States. The court held that the federal court was a court of the state. The court held that appellants were not denied due process because appellee had no duty to look beyond the nolo contendere plea to determine the substance of the allegations. The court also held that appellants' violation of 18 U.S.C.S. § 1001 fit within the phrase "other offense" as used in § 441(b).


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of appellee State Real Estate Commission that revoked appellant real estate brokers' licenses because appellants violated the Real Estate Brokers License Act of 1929 when they entered nolo contendere pleas to three criminal charges of submitting false statements in federal court.
